<p>'You must remember, darling, that I did not volunteer the proposal,
if you can give it<pb n="89     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> so definite a
name. You asked me to tell you how, in my opinion, you could best
serve the Cause, and I answered as directly and truthfully as I knew
how; but since the idea is so repellent to you, let us never return to
it.'</p>
<p>There is a short interval of awkward silence, ended by the younger
woman breaking into apologetic speech.</p>
<p>'You make me feel as if I were such a moral coward! I dare say that
my objection to your plan was only due to personal distaste, shrinking
from the disagreeable. You must own that at the first blush it had a
little look of treachery. Will you let me think it over, and try to
disentangle the merely personal motive from the other? I confess it is
not a pleasant idea to me; but, after all'--reflectively--'it is not
the pleasant that I have come to seek.'</p>
<p>The last clause of this sentence is scarcely susceptible of a
flattering interpretation as<pb n="90     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> regards
Miss Bateson's surroundings; but the latter is so much relieved by
the, at least, partially restored docility of her catechumen that she
does not quarrel with it.</p></div>

<pb n="[91]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="5"><head>CHAPTER V.</head>
<p>THE joint establishment in More Mansions is now five days
old. Althea has discovered that many things, which she has hitherto
considered as much a matter of course as the diurnal revolution of the
earth, are for the future only to be looked upon as delightful and
unexpected accidents, or as to be done without altogether. She has
discovered how very late a general servant can get up in the morning;
how very cold a hot bath can be; and how crumpled a tablecloth. She is
also in a position to decide between the comparative claims to victory
over the nose of the two detestable smells of water spilt on<pb
n="92     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> a stove, and of paraffin slopped over a
cheap lamp. Her diet, since her rebellious palate is not yet tamed to
accept the alternate and sometimes mingled greases, rawnesses, and
burnings of Eliza's infant art, over which Faustina's rides serenely
victorious, has been chiefly that of a monkey in the tropics--viz.,
oranges, bananas, and cocoa-nuts. Since at the end of nearly a week of
this innutritious fare she is not in perceptibly worse case than at
the beginning, she makes the reflection how grossly she must have
overeaten herself during the whole of her former life.</p>
<p>As to Faustina, she belongs to that class of persons--there is a
large one--to whom the minor discomforts of life are matters of
absolute indifference. Her iron health and steel nerves enable her to
face almost any kind of food without aversion; nor is it apparently of
the least moment to her whether the spoon with which she sups her
porridge is misty or bright.</p><pb n="93     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Once or twice, it is true, she has broken into tender expletives of
admiration at the heroism with which her friend braves the change for
the worse in her material conditions; but these expressions have
always been attended with an implication that to one cast in Althea's
mould the material 'worse' is more than balanced by the moral
'better.'</p>
<p>Once or twice she has also given utterance to a slight intention of
'sacking' Eliza if she does not improve. But though this condition of
her stay is never fulfilled, that unsuccessful artiste stays on. Were
she a good cook, indeed, her powers would be severely tried by the
erratic nature of the times and seasons to which she has to subdue her
art. In 4, More Mansions no food either is or is supposed to be served
at any particular hour. The dinner which on Monday is prematurely
snatched between two meetings is on Tuesday prorogued to midnight,
after a concert or dramatic entertainment at a people's hall, or<pb
n="94     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> some heated political or social platform
work at a federated women's club.</p>
<p>The project of social utility for Althea has, to her relief, not
again been broached; but she cannot reproach herself with having been
idle. In the short and breathless intervals of their public
appearances she has 'devilled' incessantly for Faustina, the heat of
her zeal more than making up for any lack of practice. She has been
the means of spreading a great deal of inflammatory literature,
against which, if her taste revolts, her sense of blazing indignation
at the abuses forcibly, if somewhat scurrilously, lashed carries her
triumphantly through. Occasionally, it is true, she utters a
hesitating protest.</p>
<p>'Do you think we need be <hi rend="italic">quite</hi> so abusive?' she asks, pausing over
a sentence even more violently vituperative than its predecessors.</p>
<p>'One cannot cure a gangrene with rose-water,' replies Miss Bateson
forcibly.</p><pb n="95     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'True, but'--still more tentatively--'do not you think our
arguments are weighty enough in themselves to be even more effective
if put temperately?'</p>
<p>'No great battle was ever won with wooden swords or
pea-shooters.'</p>
<p>'What a born fighter you are, Faustina!' says Althea, leaning back
for a moment's rest in her chair, and looking with a half-amused and
yet whole-hearted admiration up at her companion. 'No doubt you are
right--you who have given up your whole life to fight this Hydra. It
was a <hi rend="italic">grand</hi> thing to do'--her voice slightly quivering in the ardour
of her affectionate homage.</p>
<p>'It is not grand when you cannot help doing a thing. My heart
burned within me, as the old Book says; and, grand or no, it is an
easy thing to do, now that I have you to support me with your
exquisite faith and courage, after having worked alone all my
life.'</p><pb n="96     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Alone!</hi> But you had Miss Lewis.'</p>
<p>'She was a faddist; she went her own selfish way. I never was so
disillusioned about anyone in my life.'</p>
<p>Althea pauses, once again, in spite of herself, jarred.</p>
<p>'How soon do you expect to be disillusioned about me?'</p>
<p>'How soon? <hi rend="italic">When all the seas run dry</hi>.'</p>
<p>Such a declaration cannot help but be followed by an embrace, and
then they return to business.</p>
<p>'Now that you have given me the heads, told me the sense in which
you wish these letters answered, I can get through them perfectly well
by myself. I am really growing quite expert with the typewriter. How
long do you expect to be away?'</p>
<p>'You may be quite sure as short a time as I possibly can'--using
the tone with which in old days that contemptible survival, a man<pb
n="97     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> in love, was wont to part from his
mistress.</p>
<p>'I would take you with me, only that--'</p>
<p>'Only that what?'</p>
<p>'I think it might be premature; as I have explained to you, the
handful of friends I am going to meet and I are in the habit of
dealing with a class of subjects which, though they need airing badly,
I think you are as yet scarcely ripe for the discussion of.'</p>
<p>'I am ripening very fast. Well, I am willing to abide by your
judgment.'</p>
<p>With an emotional encomium on her sweet persuadeableness, Miss
Bateson bids her friend farewell; and Althea settles down without an
instant's delay to the typewriter.</p><milestone unit="section"
rend="stars"/>
<p>Two hours later a man rings the bell of No. 4. One would have
thought that, if he were an intending caller upon Miss Bateson, he
might have spared himself the trouble of the climb after seeing the
'Out' appended to<pb n="98     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> her name
downstairs. Yet it cannot be to Miss Vane that he means to pay that
civility; at least, there is no look of recognition on her face when
she appears in the doorway in answer to his summons. But, then, her
whole manner is so <hi rend="italic">boulevers&#x00E9;</hi>, her
expression one of such preoccupied consternation, that it is quite
possible she might have failed to recognize her own nearest
relatives.</p>
<p>'I beg your pardon'--taking off his hat with a very well-bred
air--'I must apologize for my intrusion; but though I saw that Miss
Bateson was out, I thought I might leave a message with her
servant.'</p>
<p>'The <hi rend="italic">servant</hi>!'--regarding him with a
distraught look. 'Something has happened to her; she has been taken
suddenly ill.'</p>
<p>'Indeed!'</p>
<p>'I was afraid to leave her, or would have sent the porter for a
doctor.'</p>
<p>'Could I be of any use?'</p>
<p>'Oh, thank you'--with an eyebeam of<pb n="99     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
heartfelt relief and gratitude--'indeed you could.'</p>
<p>'Is it Sarah?'</p>
<p>'No; Sarah left a week ago.'</p>
<p>He smiles slightly--a smile which, were she less flurried, might
convey to her mind that the tenure of domestic service in More
Mansions was not apt to be a long one.</p>
<p>'I was writing in the drawing-room, when I heard a loud noise as of
something very heavy falling. You know that one hears everything very
plainly in these flats, and I rushed into the kitchen, and found her
lying on the floor, with her head under the table.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">With her head under the table?</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes; I think it must be a fit; but, as I have never seen a person
in a fit, I cannot be sure.'</p>
<p>She is speaking very rapidly, and her troubled eye casts at him a
hurried look of inquiry as to whether he may be better informed in
this branch of science than she.</p>
<p>'May I come in and have a look at her?<pb n="100     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> I might lift her up, and, whatever ails her, I am sure her
head ought not to be left under the table.'</p>
<p>They have so far been standing on the threshold, Althea with the
door in her hand; but she now joyfully gives ground, and, fully
admitting her deliverer, leads him with precipitate steps to the scene
of the tragedy. The kitchen--a cupboard in size--is seen, when they
reach it, to be nearly filled by the prone body of a woman, who is
stretched flat upon the tiles. From under the table proceed stertorous
sounds, which prove that at least she is not dead.</p>
<p>'She has been making those dreadful noises ever since I first found
her,' says Althea in an agitated voice.</p>
<p>Her companion's answer is first to stoop over, then kneel down on
one knee beside, the object of their attention. He lifts her head
carefully, and looks scrutinizingly into the flushed and disfigured
features.</p><pb n="101     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Is</hi> it a fit?' asks the girl in an
awestruck whisper.</p>
<p>He shakes his head, and, replacing the dishevelled head on the
floor, rises again to his feet.</p>
<p>'You need not make yourself uneasy; there is nothing the matter
with her.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Nothing the matter with her?</hi>'</p>
<p>'Nothing, beyond being dead drunk.'</p>
<p>Once again, in defiance of good manners, Althea repeats his words,
but this time accompanied by a start of shocked horror.</p>
<p>'Dead drunk! But those awful noises she is making?'</p>
<p>'They are only snores.' She is struck dumb. 'Did you never see a woman under
the inspiration of gin before?' he asks, with an accent of interested
curiosity.</p>
<p>'No--yes.--I suppose so, in the street.'</p>
<p>'I have seen a good many.'</p>
<p>'What <hi rend="italic">am</hi> I to do with her?' gazing down in
stupefaction at the vanquished votary of<pb n="102     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> alcohol. 'I do not know when--it may be quite late--Faus--Miss Bateson will be back.'</p>
<p>'If you will allow me, I will carry this woman into her bedroom and
lay her on her bed to sleep it off. She will be all right when she
wakes.'</p>
<p>'Oh, <hi rend="italic">would</hi> you? I <hi
rend="italic">should</hi> be grateful! But can you manage it alone, without help? Let me lift her feet.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Pray</hi> do not touch her!'--hastily--'I am
quite up to carrying her. She will not be heavy. These sort of women
never are.'</p>
<p>He is as good as his word, and, having fished out and grasped with
adroit strength the recumbent Eliza, bears her in triumph to her
bower. Though of a wizened, East-End type, she is, like any other
perfectly inert mass, a good weight, and for a minute after laying her
down he draws his breath a little hard.</p>
<p>'I am afraid you found her very heavy?'</p>
<p>'Not at all, thank you.'</p>
<p>'And you think'--looking at the still<pb n="103     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> snoring heap with an expression in which the compassion
tries conscientiously to master the disgust, and is not completely
victorious--'that when she wakes she will be all right?'</p>
<p>'In all probability.'</p>
<p>'But supposing that she is not all right? That when she wakes up
she is still intoxicated? and that she tries to set fire to the flat,
or something of the sort? If Miss Bateson is not come back, if I am
alone, how shall I be able to cope with her?'</p>
<p>'She will probably not stir before to-morrow morning; but, if you
would allow me, I could obviate any danger of the kind you fear by
staying with you--remaining here till Miss Bateson's return.'</p>
<p>His proposal makes her look at him--she can scarcely be said to have
done so before--in order to see whether the source from which this
suggestion flows makes it seem a preferable one to the alternative of
a <hi
rend="italic">t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;-&#x00E0;-t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;</hi><pb
n="104     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> with Eliza. Unless 'burglar' or 'murderer'
be written in letters of fire upon the brow of the proposer, it can
scarcely be a less desirable one. Apparently her eyes find no such
prohibitory sentence inscribed, for she answers without any
perceptible hesitation:</p>
<p>'It would be an act of real Christian charity. But are you sure
that it is not putting you to inconvenience?--that you can spare the
time?'</p>
<p>'Perfectly sure.'</p>
<p>She throws what he thinks, what most people would think, an
extremely pretty look of silent gratitude at him, and after a moment
says interrogatively:</p>
<p>'We need not stay here, need we? In her present state she cannot do
any harm ?'</p>
<p>'None.'</p>
<p>'And the walls are so thin that we should hear in an instant if she
stopped snoring?'</p>
<p>'Should we?'</p>
<p>Without more delay, she leads him away<pb n="105     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> into the drawing-room. At her invitation he sits down. She
does the same, and at once, for the first time, they both begin to
feel shy. To neither of them is it a very usual sensation.  Althea has
lived in 'the world' all her life, and that one scanning look she had
cast at him but now has revealed to her that, if one can trust to
appearances, so has he. He is quite aware, with a tickling inward
amusement, that he had been weighed in the balance against a drunken
cook; but he feels no resentment. It is impossible since they have
probably a long spell of each other's undiluted company ahead of them,
that they can content themselves with a reciprocal silent
appraising. They must find a topic of conversation; but in their
absolute ignorance of each other, an ignorance which extends even to
their very names, what can it be? With the superior ready-wittedness
of woman, Althea hits upon one.</p><pb n="106     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You will excuse my asking, but are you by any chance the editor of
the <hi rend="italic">Firebrand?</hi>'</p>
<p>They seem fated to re echo each other's utterances:</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">The editor of the "Firebrand"!</hi> Well, no, I
am not.'</p>
<p>In answering he has, or seems to have, flushed slightly, a
transient heat of complexion which in a moment fades into a smile, but
which tells her that her 'hit' has been anything but a 'palpable'
one.</p>
<p>'Might I ask you in return why you thought I was?'</p>
<p>'My reason was a ridiculously inadequate one'--the flush is hers
now. 'You said that you had a message to leave for Miss Bateson; and
before she went out she said she hoped the--the person we are speaking
of would not call in her absence; so I put two and two together.'</p>
<p>'When one does that, my experience is that they almost always make
<hi rend="italic">five</hi>.'</p><pb n="107     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'They evidently have in this case.'</p>
<p>He seems glad of an excuse to laugh--a laugh which takes him
helplessly, like a cough, at intervals throughout the following hour,
and which he vainly tries to explain away.</p>
<p>She endeavours with equal futility to palliate her mistake.</p>
<p>'I need not tell you that I have never seen Mr.-- I do not even
know his name. Miss Bateson always speaks of him by a--a
sobriquet.'</p>
<p>'Yes, I know she does.'</p>
<p>His eye rests on the typewriter, and thence flashes back for an
instant to Althea's hatless head, drawing the obvious induction from
both.</p>
<p>'You are staying with Miss Bateson?'</p>
<p>'I am living with her.'</p>
<p>'Oh, indeed!'</p>
<p>It is clear that he is trying to keep his words politely
colourless, but interested enlightenment will pierce through their
neutral<pb n="108     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> tint, so much so that Althea
cannot forbear putting a question in her turn.</p>
<p>'Did you know my--my predecessor, Miss Lewis?'</p>
<p>Again that recurrent, helpless laugh seems inclined to master him,
but instead he masters it.</p>
<p>'Oh, rather! I beg your pardon--yes, I did know that lady.'</p>
<p>Miss Vane turns it over in her mind whether it would be strictly
honourable to the absent to ask this young man what her forerunner--a
forerunner whose light had evidently gone out in darkness, and about
whom Faustina maintains for the most part a reticence divined to be
hostile--was like. She decides that it would not.</p>
<p>'I not only knew Miss Lewis, but <hi rend="italic">her</hi> predecessor.'</p>
<p>'<hi rned="italic">Had</hi> she a predecessor?'</p>
<p>'Oh yes, more than one.'</p>
<p>Althea starts slightly. She feels as if a<pb n="109     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> sharp pebble had hit her-small, but unexpected. It takes
her a moment or two to recover.</p>
<p>'You are evidently an old acquaintance of Miss Bateson.'</p>
<p>'Very old. I have known her since I was in petticoats. Has she
never mentioned me to you?'</p>
<p>'She <hi rend="italic">may</hi> have done'--a tiny smile turning up
the corners of her mouth--'but you must remember that I--'</p>
<p>'Of course--of course! May I give you my card?'</p>
<p>It is a nice and difficult feat in the lesser manners to inform
yourself as to a person under his or her very nose, but Althea,
though it makes her feel shy, does it gracefully.</p>
<p>'Thank you'--laying down the card on the table beside her, her
consciousness enriched by the knowledge that she is in the company of
Mr. John Trecothick Drake.</p>
<p>'My name is Althea Vane.'</p><pb n="110     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>This is good as far as it goes, but at first it does not seem going
to take them much further. In her world Althea has met Drakes, and
since her first impression that he belongs to the same world as
herself has now grown to conviction, he has no doubt come across Vanes
there; but how he may be related to <hi rend="italic">her</hi> Drakes
is as obscure to her as what affinity she may have to <hi rend="italic">his</hi> Vanes is to him.</p>
<p>After a moment she begins, with delicate subtlety that yet looks
simple, to explore further.</p>
<p>'You have a West-Country sound.'</p>
<p>'Yes, I come from Devonshire.'</p>
<p>'So does Miss Bateson. One always'--smiling--'has the silly notion
that two people who inhabit the same county or continent must live
cheek by jowl.'</p>
<p>'That is exactly how we did live. Miss Bateson is the daughter
of--of one of our nearest neighbours.'</p><pb n="111     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Oh-h!'</p>
<p>The 'Oh-h!' is thoughtful, lengthened, and expresses enlightenment.
If her <hi rend="italic">vis-&#x00E0;-vis</hi>, with his high nose,
his admirable coat, and faultless utterance, differ strangely from
such of Faustina's men friends as have hitherto met Althea's eye and
ear--friends whose speech is either heavily bebrogued or gives that
supremacy which it has lately gained among the masses to the vowel <hi
rend="italic">I</hi>--the explanation lies in the fact of their
having sported together in childhood among the Devonshire
buttercups. The thought had certainly crossed her mind--instantly and
remorsefully chased away for its unworthiness--that he is too much
like a gentleman to be an intimate of Faustina's.</p>
<p>'Then, you know her family?'</p>
<p>'Oh yes, of course.'</p>
<p>'They are, I believe, not--not at all worthy of her?'</p>
<p>'Has she told you so?'</p><pb n="112     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'No-o--oh no, certainly not. She would not condescend to say
anything in detraction of them beyond--beyond--'</p>
<p>He waits, politely expectant, but not helping her to a word, as he
might so easily do.</p>
<p>She has to set off upon a remodelled sentence:</p>
<p>'I gathered it from the fact of her having had to leave home
through her faithfulness to her convictions. If the species of
persecution to which she was exposed--'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Persecution!</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes, persecution'--firmly.</p>
<p>He looks upon the floor, and once again she has reason to suspect
that he is struggling with a laugh.</p>
<p>'They certainly did not hit it off particularly well.'</p>
<p>The entire lack of fervour in this utterance brings the blood to
her face.</p>
<p>'As far as I have heard, light and darkness never have hit it off
particularly well since the world began.'</p><pb n="113     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>He lifts his downcast orbs, and looks at her with a pleased
gravity.</p>
<p>'Miss Bateson and I are companions in iniquity,' he says
deliberately. 'If her family are not worthy of her, neither are mine
of me.'</p>
<p>She glances at him with a quickened interest. Hitherto his outside
advantages have done him rather disservice than otherwise with her, as
proclaiming him to belong to that <hi rend="italic">r&#x00E9;gime</hi> which she has renounced.</p>
<p>'Do you mean--'</p>
<p>'I mean--'</p>
<p>The ting of the electric bell breaks into his answer. Faustina has
returned.</p></div>

<pb n="[114]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="6"><head>CHAPTER VI.</head>
<p>'So that is the new enthusiasm, is it?'</p>
<p>The collapse of her cook has been explained to Miss Bateson, and
has been received with that philosophic indifference by which she is
wont to baffle the lesser blows of fate.</p>
<p>'She is no loss.  Until I find another, we can turn into an A B C
for food. Is not it a blessing for me'--addressing the man--'that
Althea does not care a straw what she eats?'</p>
<p>Althea's spirit is not yet so chastened as to escape a slight prick
of indignation at hearing her Christian name thus made free with to a
perfect stranger; and partly to conceal an<pb n="115     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> irritation of which she is ashamed, partly out of
delicacy, she leaves the two friends together.</p>
<p>The man lights a cigarette, and, leaning his shoulder against the
chimney-piece, gives utterance to the sentence above quoted:</p>
<p>'So that is the new enthusiasm, is it?'</p>
<p>'If you choose to put it so'--lighting up too.</p>
<p>'It is a more comprehensible ardour than the last; but if you will
excuse my putting it so, she does not look cut quite on <hi
rend="italic">our</hi> pattern.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Our!</hi>'--with a withering glance at the
elegance of his <hi rend="italic">tout ensemble</hi>.</p>
<p>'Yes, <hi rend="italic">our!</hi> I suppose I may be allowed to
have given proofs of my right of citizenship, even though a few old
clothes survive from my unregenerate state. Are you determined never
to take me to your heart until I am dressed wholly from a slop-shop?'
His tone is one of careless intimacy, slightly touched with an
inoffensive impertinence.</p><pb n="116     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'However much her outside may belie her--'</p>
<p>'I am far from objecting to it.'</p>
<p>'She <hi rend="italic">is</hi> one of us!'</p>
<p>'Is she?'</p>
<p>'She is prepared to go as far as anybody. She is very keen about
the vote, perfectly sound upon the Marriage Question, and her opinion
of men is, if possible, lower than mine.'</p>
<p>He receives this last thrust with perfect equanimity.</p>
<p>'She is a very valuable acquisition. And how long do you think she
will last?'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Last!</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes; how long before she follows poor Lewis to Limbo?'</p>
<p>The question is a provocative one; but Faustina's temper is nearly,
if not quite, up to the level of her nerves and her digestion.</p>
<p>'She asked me that question herself this very day.'</p><pb
n="117     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'And what did you answer?'</p>
<p>'It is not of the least consequence what I answered.'</p>
<p>He stands thoughtful, the end of his cigarette between his finger
and thumb.</p>
<p>'In what direction do you mean to utilize her? She does not look
much like a hewer of wood or drawer of water.'</p>
<p>'It is very kind of you to be so much interested about her. But do
not disquiet yourself; she will find her proper sphere.'</p>
<p>'What is her history? How did you get hold of her? Is she an
isolated fact? and if not, how did her relations allow you to spirit
her away?'</p>
<p>'It was no case of spiriting; she has broken with her family
deliberately for the sake of her opinions.'</p>
<p>'Like you!'</p>
<p>There is a suspicion of the same laugh as had puzzled Althea in his
voice, but Faustina<pb n="118     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> apparently does not
notice it, unless to it is due the tartness of her response.</p>
<p>'Like <hi rend="italic">you</hi>, too; only that her vocation is a
much more genuine one than yours.'</p>
<p>'Thank you.'</p>
<p>'Not that you'--relenting--'have not given some good proofs of your
sincerity.'</p>
<p>'Thank you.'</p>
<p>'But that paltry levity of yours makes one doubt that you can ever
be really in earnest about anything.'</p>
<p>'Thank you. I am growing so surfeited with sweets that I think I
shall wish you good-evening.'</p>
<p>Apparently they understand each other, for she lets him go without
remonstrance.</p>
<p>'What is the editor of the <hi rend="italic">Firebrand</hi> like?'
asks Althea that same evening, apparently apropos of nothing.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Like!</hi> How do you mean?'</p>
<p>'Like to look at. Is he prepossessing in appearance?'</p><pb
n="119     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Faustina's wide-awake eyes open even more fully than usual.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Prepossessing!</hi> Good Lord, no! Why should he be? He is a man of the
people, and he looks it. Why do you ask?'</p>
<p>'You have mentioned him so often, that I thought it would be as
well to have some idea of his appearance, in case he called when you
were out,' replies Miss Vane, not quite candidly.</p>
<p>Faustina hangs her dark head luxuriously backwards over the top of
her chair--it is one of her rare moments of inaction--and blows the
smoke of her cigarette through her nostrils.</p>
<p>'Prepossessing!' she repeats presently. 'Darling, have not you yet
learnt that we workers have no time to spare for the graces?'</p>
<p>'Then, your visitor of to-day'--a slight slowness in bringing out
the query--'is evidently not a worker?'</p><pb n="120     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'He looked a preposterous dandy,' replies Miss Bateson, with a
scorn that yet sounds lenient; 'but then, as you know, the habits of a
lifetime are not shaken off in a day; and it is Sunday, isn't it? Oh
yes! the bells here in London never give one a chance of forgetting
that fact. But despite his silly fopperies, there is stuff--yes, real
stuff--in John Drake.'</p>
<p>'How has he shown it?'</p>
<p>Faustina sits up, as if to give a more marked emphasis to her
reply.</p>
<p>'By chucking twenty thousand pounds a year.' Althea has sat up,
too, her eyes alight with coming admiration. 'His father owns a
chemical factory in the East End, and when John found the poisonous
conditions under which the hands spent their lives, he refused to
touch a penny of money wrung from the wretchedness of hundreds of his
fellow-creatures; and as his father entirely declined to listen to any
suggestions for bettering<pb n="121     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> those
conditions, he threw up the whole thing, and old Trecothick has since
absolutely disinherited him.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Trecothick!</hi> I thought his name was <hi
rend="italic">Drake!</hi>'</p>
<p>'His mother was Drake; and the old sweater was so pleased at
having married into one the best Devonshire families that he took her
name.'</p>
<p>'Twenty thousand a year!' repeats Althea in an awed voice. 'How
magnificent! And what injustice one does people.'</p>
<p>'do not fall into the other extreme, dearest, ad make a hero of
him! He is still better off than five-sixths of the human race. His
mother's money--she died when he was a child--came to him. It amounts
to several hundreds a year.'</p>
<p>'Several hundreds! But he gave up many thousands!'</p>
<p>'Yes, he did; and I am sure I have no wish no minimize the
sacrifice. I only wanted<pb n="122     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> to guard you
against your generous tendency to idealize--a tendency by which I have
so magnificently profited.'</p>
<p>'He hinted at some vital difference of opinion with his family; but
I never, never dreamed--' She breaks off.</p>
<p>'What did he tell you? In what connection did he introduce the
subject?'</p>
<p>'We were talking of you.'</p>
<p>'Of <hi rend="italic">me?</hi>'--a little sharply.</p>
<p>'He was saying that he had been wronged by his family in the same
way as you had been by yours.'</p>
<p>Faustina's cheek-bones take on for a second a dim, dark flush.</p>
<p>'Did he tell you anything more about me?'</p>
<p>' He said that you were the daughter of one of his nearest
neighbours.'</p>
<p>The flush pales into a relieved, dry smile. 'That was a
euphemism. I am the daughter of old Trecothick's bailiff--none of
your gentlemen bailiffs: a common working farmer.<pb n="123     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> I wonder that John Drake has not known me long enough to
know that I glory in the class from which I spring. If I were not a
working woman by necessity, I should certainly be one by choice.'</p>
<p>Althea acknowledges this noble sentiment by an appreciative look;
but that her thoughts are still rather with the absent hero than the
present heroine is made plain by her next words:</p>
<p>'Has he any profession?'</p>
<p>'I believe he used to suppose that he hung about the Law Courts,
but his real work is in connection with the Settlement down at Canning
Town. He lives there for months at a time, organizing meetings,
giving lectures, and so forth.'</p>
<p>'Appearances are deceitful,' says Althea, with soft
thoughtfulness. 'He does not look like it.'</p>
<p>'Probably he thinks that you do not look like it, either.'</p><pb
n="124     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>The topic drops; but it gives Althea a new feeling towards the
subject of it when next he appears on her horizon.</p>
<p>'Darling,' says Faustina, one morning after the union of the
friends has lasted for a month, 'did not you tell me that you wished
to visit your family? Would not to-day be a good opportunity? I could
spare you better to-day than most days, because I have a person coming
to speak to me on business in the afternoon?'</p>
<p>'Business that I am not to hear?' replies Althea, with affectionate
playfulness at the absurdity of the idea.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Business that you are not to hear!</hi> How can
you be so absurd? My beloved'--seeing a look of unaffected surprise on
Althea's face at the unwonted tartness of her tone--'you make me wince
when you hint at such a possibility as any concealment between us, even in play.'</p>
<p>'To-day will suit me admirably. Dear<pb n="125     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> things!'--with an accent of hesitating tenderness. 'I do
not know whether they will care to see me; but I shall be very, <hi
rend="italic">very</hi> glad to see them again.'</p>
<p>Faustina turns away, having summoned up an expression of suffering
to her strong face.</p>
<p>'You need not be jealous,' says the other, laying a reassuring hand
upon her companion's shoulder. 'Fondly as I love them, I still think I
have chosen the better part.'</p>
<p>She says it with conviction--says it over to herself on her
way--even while little waves of expectant, if rather nervous,
pleasure keep running over her--even when, from the top of her bus,
she sees Aunt Lavinia rolling along Piccadilly in her victoria,
unconscious of the eye of her <hi
rend="italic">d&#x00E9;class&#x00E9;</hi> young relative stooping
admiringly, yet not enviously, down upon the feathers in her bonnet
and the little coal-black toy Spitz by her side from her vulgar
eyrie.</p><pb n="126     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>When the bus stops, she steps gingerly down the dirty stair,
anxiously guarding her skirts.</p>
<p>She has dressed carefully, being anxious not to prejudice her
family still further against the line of life she has adopted by any
deterioration in her appearance.</p>
<p>There is still a short distance to be walked before reaching the
house, in a good Mayfair street, which the William Botelers have taken
on lease. The William Botelers! How hard it is to picture Clare as one
half of 'the William Botelers'!</p>
<p>As she nears her goal, misgivings get the upper hand of hope in her
breast. What sort of a welcome will she get? She has come unasked.</p>
<p>After all, how little notice they have taken of her since the
schism that separated her from them! She has written three--or is it
four?--times to Edward, and been answered--for it <hi
rend="italic">is</hi> an answer--by blank silence.</p><pb n="127
DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Fanny has sent her nothing but the conventional love that nobody
gives and nobody cares to take, in Clare's last letter; and
Clare!--Clare's two letters have had that aroma of sweet, tactful
kindness which breathes from all her gracious words and deeds; but,
oh, how unlike they have been to the close-scribbled outpourings of
her girlhood, when the sisters happened to be parted for even a day!
In these she has 'writ large,' to hide the poverty of her topics, and
even so has had to swell one starved page by comments on a political
incident. Two years ago, what world-convulsion not affecting their two
selves would have found a place in their crowded pages?</p>
<p>She has reached the door, and her heart beats quickly as she
rings. How many of these now-alarming dear ones will she have to face?
William--the excellent, insufferable William--will, thank God! be
certainly at the Stock Exchange; Edward at Balliol,<pb n="128     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> Thomas at Eton. It is only gentle Clare and childish Fanny
whom she is needlessly bracing her nerves to meet. Yet the trepidation
of her spirit does not subside as she sits in the empty drawing-room,
while the butler goes in search of his mistress.</p>
<p>The room is softly brilliant in dazzlingly clean paint and gilding,
delicate pompadour satin hangings, wedding-presents, and countless
flowers. It strikes Althea, as she sits there, how little time it
takes one entirely to change one's standpoint in life. It is scarcely
five weeks since she left civilization, and yet it is with something
of the wondering stare of an inhabitant of Poplar or Stratford that
she is surveying the pretty luxuries of her sister's room. She has
seen scores of such rooms, and knows that there exist in London tens
of thousands of them, though perhaps, as a rule, not quite so clean,
since it is only a small minority that have been freshly decorated by
an ardent bridegroom for his bride.</p><pb n="129     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Thee!</hi> this <hi rend="italic">is</hi>
nice!'</p>
<p>Clare has entered without her visitor hearing her step, and in a
second her warm arms are round the runagate. With a sort of sob in her
throat the latter realizes that Clare, at all events, is all
right.</p>
<p>'Will you have me to luncheon?'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Will I?</hi>'</p>
<p>'And shall I have the luck to keep you to myself?'</p>
<p>The other hesitates.</p>
<p>'Fanny is here, of course.'</p>
<p>'Has Fanny begun to count? She never used to do so.'</p>
<p>'And Ned is up for the night.'</p>
<p>'Dear old Ned! How glad I shall be to see him!'</p>
<p>The ejaculation is a quavering one, and falls rather flat.</p>
<p>And I have invited a girl, a friend of his, at his request, to meet
him.'</p>
<p>'A girl? Oh!'</p><pb n="130     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'A very nice girl--Miss Delafield.'</p>
<p>'Lady Lanington's daughter?'</p>
<p>'Yes; do not you remember her ball last year, when the electric
light went out?'</p>
<p>'Perfectly.'</p>
<p>For a minute silence falls between them, Althea, and probably
Clare, too, musing upon the gulf that parts them from that darkened
entertainment.</p>
<p>'How pretty your house is!'</p>
<p>'It will be, I hope. We are rather in the rough still.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">In the rough!</hi>'--smiling sardonically.</p>
<p>There is perhaps something unintentionally challenging in Miss
Vane's tone, for her sister looks frightened.</p>
<p>'I dare say the expression applied to a room like this sounds
ridiculously affected to you, who have been seeing so much of the
"seamy side" of life. You would'--with an apprehensive glance towards
the door--'hardly have time to tell me anything about<pb n="131
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> it before luncheon--I mean, it would not be worth
while to begin, would it?'</p>
<p>'Do not be afraid. I am not going to begin.'</p>
<p>She says it with a laugh, but it is a mirth that covers a good deal
of wounded feeling. They are both relieved at Fanny's entrance. Fanny
is quite glad to see Althea; and so she is to see the two
luncheon-seeking young men who presently appear; so she is to see Miss
Delafield. The latter is one of those lofty-statured,
porcelain-textured, exquisitely groomed young creatures who may be
seen on any fine morning, between February and August, in considerable
numbers, doing infinite credit to their country in the shops and on
the pavement of Sloane Street.</p>
<p>Sisters know each other terribly well, and it is obvious to the
intruding one that Mrs. Boteler's anxiety as to herself is heightened
since the arrival of 'Edward's friend.' Her look travels oftener
doorwards, and presently<pb n="132     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> Althea sees
her slip quietly out of the room. She knows as well as if she had been
told in words that Clare has heard Edward's foot on the stairs, and is
hasting to tell him of the culprit's presence, so that his jaw may
not drop too perceptibly on catching sight of her.</p>
<p>The precaution is not so very successful, after all. Nothing can be
more chilling than the eye and hand with which he salutes her. She
feels so hurt and mortified that, when they go down to luncheon, she
chooses a seat as far from him as the size of the table will
admit.</p>
<p>She finds herself beside one of the other young men. She knows him
slightly, but he is so entirely in the dark as to her present mode of
life, so determined that she is living with her sister, it is so
impossible to enlighten him without annoying her family by her
revelation, that their talk is one series of misunderstandings on his
part, and parrying awkward questions on hers. He cannot<pb n="133
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> think what has happened to her, and, as soon as
courtesy will admit, turns with relief to his other neighbour,
Fanny. Upon the sunny brooklet of her small glib talk, vaguely
flattering, as every man who converses with her feels, though none
could explain why, Althea presently sees and hears him sail away
twenty knots an hour.</p>
<p>Since the chair on her left is filled by an old cousin of William
Boteler's, who had come in late, and is too much occupied pouring
scraps of Boteler family news into Clare's attentive ear to notice
her, she is left to the enjoyment of her luncheon, which seems to her
extraordinarily delicious. She reproaches herself for the acute
pleasure her palate derives from it, contrasting herself with
Faustina.</p>
<p>After the ladies have returned to the drawing-room, she finds Miss
Delafield accosting her, and civilly recalling herself to her
memory.</p><pb n="134     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>They are both still standing, when, the men having immediately
followed them, Edward makes straight as a die for the little group. At
the same moment the youth who had so resolvedly misunderstood Althea
at luncheon asks Miss Delafield a question, and she, turning a little
to answer it, leaves the brother and sister <hi
rend="italic">t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;-&#x00E0;-t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;</hi>.</p>
<p>'Can you spare me a little bit of notice from metal more
attractive?' Althea asks in a friendly if rather nervous low
voice.</p>
<p>'Yes,' he answers; 'I wish to speak to you. Would you mind coming
into the back drawing-room for a moment?'</p>
<p>She gives glad assent, and follows him.</p>
<p>'I <hi rend="italic">am</hi> flattered,' she says, with a slight
meaning smile thrown back towards the room they have left. 'This <hi
rend="italic">is</hi> a compliment! Dear old boy! how pleasant it is
to see you again!'</p>
<p>When you have led a person apart with no other design than to
administer to him or<pb n="135     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> her a pungent
snub, it is awkward to have the conversation opened in such a spirit
as this by the intended recipient, and for a moment Edward is taken
aback.</p>
<p>'I will not keep you a moment,' he says in half-apology; 'I only
want to ask a favour of you.'</p>
<p>'A favour?'</p>
<p>'Yes, a favour. I saw you just now in conversation with Miss
Delafield.'</p>
<p>'Why should not I be in conversation with her?'</p>
<p>He is silent.</p>
<p>'It was she who addressed me, not I her.'</p>
<p>'I am not finding fault with you. You have, of course, a perfect
right to talk to whom you choose. What I was going to ask you was, as
I told you, a favour.'</p>
<p>'What favour?'</p>
<p>Her smile has died away, and her voice is dry and hard.</p><pb
n="136     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'It is only that in any future conversation you may have with
her--'</p>
<p>'I have not the slightest desire to have any future conversations
with her.'</p>
<p>He reddens.</p>
<p>'I dare say not. I do not think that you would have much in
common.'</p>
<p>'She asked me whether I remembered the electric light going out at
their ball last year, and I said "Yes. I did."'</p>
<p>'All I wished to ask you was that, in case you did talk to her, you
would refrain from airing your peculiar views to her.'</p>
<p>Althea turns pale and bites her lip, but the action does not
succeed in keeping in the gibing answer:</p>
<p>'You are behind the times. Do not you know that philanthropy is the
<hi rend="italic">fashion?</hi>'</p>
<p>His retort is not less gibing:</p>
<p>'Philanthropy! Yes; I was not alluding to philanthropy.'</p></div>

<pb n="[137]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="7"><head>CHAPTER VII.</head>
<p>THE rest of the party have dispersed, and Althea sits on a sofa
beside Clare, her eyes brimming with angry tears. Miss Delafield,
their innocent occasion, has, in going away, under Edward's very nose,
asked for her address, and for leave to call upon her, and she has
bungled and stammered in her efforts to evade the little civility. Her
wounded spirit would have carried her out of the house at once had not
Clare, by an imploring sign, urged her to stay. Mrs. Boteler had seen
the expression of the two faces on their return from their trip to the
back drawing-room, and is now engaged in pouring balm<pb n="138
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> into the hurts of the worst mauled of the two
combatants.</p>
<p>'He is in love; people in love are always unjust.'</p>
<p>'He spoke to me in a way that was perfectly unjustifiable.'</p>
<p>'Did he? He always <hi rend="italic">was</hi> rather peppery; but I
think he wanted to make you an <hi rend="italic">amende</hi>. He would
have liked to shake hands with you, only that you turned so resolutely
away.'</p>
<p>'And now, perhaps, he will be killed in a railway accident going
back to Oxford,' says Althea lugubriously, one large tear bursting
from its dyke and running down her nose.</p>
<p>Clare laughs.</p>
<p>'That <hi rend="italic">is</hi> piling on the agony!'</p>
<p>'What harm did he suppose I should do the girl ?'--with a fresh
burst of indignation.</p>
<p>'Perhaps'--hesitatingly--'he was a little afraid that you might
inoculate her with your views of marriage.'</p><pb n="139     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'What does he know about my views of marriage? He has never had the
fairness to let me state them.'</p>
<p>'Do not you think that, if two people know that they differ
fundamentally upon a subject, silence is the wisest course?'</p>
<p>'No, I do not; I like fresh air. I think that there is no subject
that is not the better for ventilation.'</p>
<p>Mrs. Boteler gives a slight inward shudder. There is such a whiff
of Faustina about this last sentence. It takes a minute to conquer her
repulsion. Before she can ask, 'You go on liking your life?' Althea
has captured her errant teardrop, and her eyes sparkle bright and
dry.</p>
<p>'It is hardly a question of liking. If you mean, do I still think I
have chosen wisely, I answer emphatically, in spite of you all, in
spite of Ned'--faltering--'"Yes, I do."'</p>
<p>Clare looks at her wistfully. She would<pb n="140     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> like to put a great many questions as to the details of
that life which has thinned her sister's face, and yet lit it with
such a fire of enthusiasm; but the intense distaste which she shares
with the rest of her family for alluding, even obliquely, to Miss
Bateson keeps her silent.</p>
<p>'You have grown thin!'</p>
<p>'Have I? That only proves that I added superfluous flesh to all my
other superfluities.'</p>
<p>Altogether it is not a great success, though Clare at parting gives
her a close, sisterly hug, and says ruefully:</p>
<p>'I do not like to let you go. I want to keep you and fatten you
up. I do not believe that that wo--I mean, I am sure you have not
enough to eat.'</p>
<p>It is with a lump in her throat that Althea, from the summit of her
return bus--she has grown in the last five weeks a past-mistress in
the colours of those puzzling vehicles--<pb n="141     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>reflects upon her family. How nice they all looked--how
much handsomer than she had remembered them! and how well they do
without her!</p>
<p>They did not ask her one question as to the great and heart-rending
subjects which have burnt all other and lesser interests out of her
own life. They did not show, because they did not feel, the least
concern for the tens of thousands of stunted, starved, and poisoned
lives running parallel to their own wadded satin ones.</p>
<p>What tales she could have told them of the hopeless women, and
dwindled little children, and famine-goaded men, to whom Faustina and
Drake have dedicated their lives! But they would not have listened to
her if she had. Edward would have--nay, but what could Edward say or do
more wounding than what, without any provocation on her part, he had
already done? And Clare would have looked alarmed, and given<pb
n="142     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> the conversation a swift, if gentle, ply
in some happier direction.</p>
<p>Her bus does not take her quite to her own Mansions; she has to
walk a few hundred yards along that mean and noisy street whose
proximity helps to bring the rents of More Mansions within indigent
means. She has got half-way through it, when she sees one of the two
persons whom she has been so favourably comparing with her own
kinsfolk coming to meet her.</p>
<p>Drake and she have been several times in each other's company since
their first informal introduction over the drunken cook's body, though
not often <hi
rend="italic">t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;-&#x00E0;-t&#x00EA;t&#x00E9;</hi>. Whenever
this has happened, there has always been on Althea's mind, and perhaps
also a little in her manner, the print of that impression which the
knowledge of his great renunciation had graved there on her first hearing it.</p>
<p>He is frowning over some disagreeable<pb n="143     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> thought when she first catches sight of him, but they meet
with two smiles.</p>
<p>'Have you been to see Miss Bateson?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'Did you find her? Oh, but of course you did. She had to stay at
home to see a person on business.'</p>
<p>'I was that person.'</p>
<p>'Were you?'</p>
<p>There is a slight inflexion of surprise in her voice at Faustina's
not having mentioned this fact; but she does not dwell upon it.</p>
<p>'You look tired.'</p>
<p>'I have been to see my family.'</p>
<p>'Is that an epigram?'</p>
<p>She laughs a little dismally.</p>
<p>'No; but they live a long way off, and my bus was a very jolting
one. I felt as if I were out hunting.'</p>
<p>'May I walk with you to your door?'</p>
<p>It is so deeply unlikely that Edward will return to Oxford
vi&#x00E2; Flood Street, Chelsea,<pb n="144     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> that
she answers, without any perceptible delay: 'Yes, do.'</p>
<p>He walks along beside her quite silently--so silently that she
wonders why he had volunteered his company. At last, when the great
pile of red brick that is to part them looms near, he speaks.</p>
<p>'Do you care to hear what my business with Miss Bateson was?'</p>
<p>'If you care to tell it me!'--surprised.</p>
<p>He still hesitates.</p>
<p>'I hope, at all events, that it was satisfactory.'</p>
<p>'It would be impossible to imagine anything less so.'</p>
<p>He pauses before adding to this vague yet emphatic statement of
failure, an apparently irrelevant question.</p>
<p>'Are you fond of asking favours? I am not. Well, I have just asked
one, and been refused.'</p>
<p>'A favour?' It is the word that has been<pb n="145     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> ringing in Althea's head since her brother's insulting
employment of it, and her forehead involuntarily contracts. 'Was it
Faustina whom you asked?'</p>
<p>Yes.'</p>
<p>'I am sure that if it had been anything possible she would have
granted it.'</p>
<p>'Are you? Why?'</p>
<p>'Because she never spares herself, and because I know what a--what a
high value she has for you.'</p>
<p>'Has she? Oh, we puff each other off when it suits us.'</p>
<p>She looks indignantly at him, but apparently he is too much
absorbed to notice it.</p>
<p>'You know her extraordinary faculty for getting up enough of a
subject that she knows nothing of to write a rousing article upon
it?'</p>
<p>'I know the clearness and strength of her mind, and her power of
picking out essentials from accessories.'</p><pb n="146     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Well'--a little impatiently--'let us, call it that; then, <hi
rend="italic">a fortiori</hi> you would think that it would be easy to
her to knock off a few pages upon a subject that she really does know
something about?'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'I have had it very much at heart that she should write me an
article upon "Dangerous Trades," and get it into the <hi
rend="italic">Universal</hi>.'</p>
<p>'And she refused?'</p>
<p>'Point-blank.'</p>
<p>'She knew that the editor would not take it.'</p>
<p>'On the contrary, I happen to know that his sympathies are warmly
with us.'</p>
<p>A wave of colour rolls over Althea's face.</p>
<p>'There must be some mistake. You know yourself that there is no
subject that she feels so strongly about, nothing that she works so
hard at, as factory legislation.'</p>
<p>'There is no mistake.' They have reached the separating-point. 'I
have lately learned<pb n="147     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> some peculiarly
grisly facts about an industry in which chromate of potash is
employed, and which I am very anxious to bring before the public.'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'The other day a friend who saw the workmen engaged in this trade
told me that the dust eats through the gristle of the nostrils, and
destroys the palate or roof of the mouth.'</p>
<p>She gives a little ejaculation of horror.</p>
<p>'He said he had seen a pencil passed through the nostril of a man
who had been employed in the trade for some years, and that it was a
certain result of a given period of work.'</p>
<p>He cannot complain that his tale is not interesting her. She has
come quite close to him; her cheeks are blanched, and her eyes are
plunged into his. Deep and genuine as his own concern in the topic is,
he cannot help the passing thought of how easily their<pb n="148
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> attitude might be misread by a passer-by.</p>
<p>'You did not tell Faustina <hi rend="italic">that?</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes, I did.'</p>
<p>'And she still refused?'</p>
<p>'As I tell you, point-blank.'</p>
<p>'There must be some mistake. You could not have made her
understand.'</p>
<p>'She understood perfectly.'</p>
<p>For a moment there is silence; then: 'You <hi
rend="italic">must</hi> be doing her an injustice,' the girl says in a
voice unsteady with emotion; 'such a refusal would run counter to the
whole tenor of her life. Will you--will you wait down here for a few
moments while I go to her and have it cleared up?'</p>
<p>He shakes his head. 'It would be useless.'</p>
<p>But she has turned from him, and is speeding up the narrow stone
stairs.</p>
<p>'How out of breath you are, my own!' says Miss Bateson, slewing
herself round from her writing-table, and dropping her pen<pb
n="149     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> to extend her arms.  But Althea neglects
their invitation.</p>
<p>'Faustina, I have just met Mr. Drake.'</p>
<p>The ecstatic smile upon Miss Bateson's lips dies away.</p>
<p>'That fact was scarcely enough to put anyone out of breath.'</p>
<p>'He has been telling me what his business with you was.'</p>
<p>'Has he?'</p>
<p>'Of the request he made you.'</p>
<p>'Indeed!'</p>
<p>'And which you refused?'</p>
<p>'I did.'</p>
<p>The calmness of this assent to what she had so passionately
disbelieved knocks Althea on her beam-ends; and this, combined with
her as yet not recovered breath, silences her, though not for
long.</p>
<p>'But did he tell you--did you take in the facts, the monstrous
facts, that he has learnt about--'</p><pb n="150     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> 
<p>'Chromate of potash?' interrupts Faustina, with a rather bored
air. 'Oh yes. After all, what is it but one more pebble upon the
gigantic cairn that is being built up against the day of
retribution?'</p>
<p>'But why did you refuse?--you, who are always foremost in the
fight?'</p>
<p>Miss Bateson's temper is good, and well in hand, but she is not
very fond of being cross-questioned.</p>
<p>'I did it for what I considered sufficient reasons.'</p>
<p>'And which you have not confidence enough in me to tell me!' cries
the other in a deeply wounded voice. But here Faustina is equal to the
occasion.</p>
<p>'If there is any question of want of confidence between us, it is
hardly on my side.'</p>
<p>She turns back to her writing-table, as if to close the subject;
but Althea is not so to be put off.</p><pb n="151     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I <hi rend="italic">had</hi> confidence in you; I told him I knew
it was not true--that there was some mistake--that it was so unlike
you. I asked him to wait until I ran up to you to have it cleared
up.'</p>
<p>Faustina lifts up an eye, in which gratification is not the
leading expression, to the acolyte thus turned judge, and surveys her
standing quivering in red-hot excitement over her.</p>
<p>'It is inexpressibly painful to me to find that you have been
discussing me with one who is, or ought to be, an almost entire
stranger to you.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Ought to be!</hi> What do you mean,
Faustina?'</p>
<p>the tone, no less than the crimsoned face, of her metamorphosed
disciple tell Miss Bateson that she has gone too far.</p>
<p>'I had thought,' she sayd, with a hint of apology, and also of a
break in her voice, 'that there was such perfect union of heart and
mind between us, that we did not need<pb n="152     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
an intruding third to explain us to one another.'</p>
<p>Althea's answer is given in company with a move towards the
door.</p>
<p>'There can be no union of heart and mind where one is shut out from
the other's confidence.'</p>
<p>But Faustina is at the door before her.</p>
<p>'My darling, if you leave me in this spirit I shall go wild with
grief. What do you ask of me? I am most willing to lay bare my heart
to you, as I have so often done before--to tell you the reasons why I
refused John Drake's request, or, rather, command--for he was
unpleasantly peremptory--to do an article for him on "Dangerous
Trades" for the <hi rend="italic">Universal</hi>.'</p>
<p>'The editor would not take it?' puts in Althea eagerly.</p>
<p>'Oh yes, he would; but--but there are other papers beside the <hi
rend="italic">Universal</hi>--other editors to be considered beside
Macbride.'</p><pb n="153     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I do not understand.'</p>
<p>Miss Bateson does not seem in any particular hurry to explain. She
clears her throat and makes one or two false starts. She gets under
way at last.</p>
<p>'It is only now and then that I get an article to do for the
<hi rend="italic">Universal</hi>, whereas I am on the staff of the <hi
rend="italic">Cheapside</hi>; in fact, I draw a considerable part of my tiny income from it.'</p>
<p>Althea looks mystified.</p>
<p>'But there is no question in this case of the <hi rend="italic">Cheapside</hi>.'</p>
<p>Faustina sighs heavily.</p>
<p>'Life is so complicated, and it is so difficult to explain its
entanglements, even to one's nearest and dearest. You know that I
depend entirely--almost entirely--on my own exertions for support; that
I neither ask nor receive any help from my family.'</p>
<p>'I know'--with an access of warmth--'it is exceedingly noble of
you.'</p><pb n="154     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Even with the prop of this plaudit Miss Bateson again
hesitates.</p>
<p>'Such being the case, to quarrel with the editor is to quarrel with
my bread-and-butter--in plain words, to give up my chief means of
subsistence.'</p>
<p>'But why should you quarrel with him?'</p>
<p>Faustina's eye wanders distressedly towards the window, whence a
squeezy pinch of the Thames is to be caught sight of, then back again,
and she takes the plunge.</p>
<p>'Because--because--nobody can deplore it more deeply than I--he
holds shares in a company concerned in that particular trade; and if I
expose its iniquities, it will naturally be prejudicial to his
interests, and he will be certain to turn me adrift.'</p>
<p>There is a dead silence. Althea's face has paled and stiffened, and
it is apparently with great difficulty that she gets out the
words:</p>
<p>'Thank you for your explanation.'</p>
<p>'You think it a satisfactory one?' cries<pb n="155     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> Miss Bateson, seizing the other's perfectly irresponsive
hand. 'You see that my reason for refusing was sound and valid?'</p>
<p>'I see,' replies the other dryly, 'that there is a wide ditch
between admiring a great sacrifice such as Mr. Drake's and emulating
it.'</p>
<p>Faustina's cheek puts on a dull flush, which shows even through her
habitual high colour, and she bites her lip; but she is still able to
keep herself in hand.</p>
<p>'It is a little hard to have John Drake set up as a model before
me--<hi rend="italic">me</hi>, who first set him on the path of
renunciation.'</p>
<p>'It was you yourself who supplied me with material for the
comparison.'</p>
<p>'There is no <hi rend="italic">real</hi> comparison between us,'
returns Faustina, drawing herself up. 'He is a blundering amateur,
with no comprehensive grasp of the subject, only a hot-headed zeal for
one or two details of it, while I--oh! is it possible that you, of all
people, should<pb n="156     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> need to be told that I
have devoted all my womanhood, every heart-beat, every pulse-throb, to
fighting the Hydra?'</p>
<p>Her tone is so lofty, and Althea feels herself being put so
completely in the wrong, that she has to use a strong effort in order
to recall the original facts of the case before she can say in a
steady, low voice:</p>
<p>'That was why it seemed to me so incredible.'</p>
<p>'One must live,' cries the other, bringing her hands together with
a melodramatic gesture. 'Cotton-wool people like you and Drake are
incapable of putting yourselves in the position of us toilers and
moilers for our daily bread. If I take pay from a man engaged in the
iniquitous traffic that my whole life is spent in making war upon, I
use it as a lever against him; do not you see that I hoist him with
his own petard?'</p>
<p>Althea shakes her head.</p><pb n="157     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'No; I do not.'</p>
<p>'Do not you see that I <hi rend="italic">must</hi> keep body and
soul together? Oh!'--with an abrupt ascent or descent from her
self-justifying tone to one of lovelorn upbraiding--'has it come to
this? After all these happy heart-to-heart weeks, am I to stand
arraigned like a criminal at the bar before you?'</p>
<p>Althea's mouth is all one painful quiver, a wave of horrid
disillusionment pouring over her.</p>
<p>'You cannot think it more dreadful than I do, a more shocking
reversal of the right order of things! Of course you must live, and no
one can admire and reverence your honourable poverty more than I do;
but--but would not it be possible for you--I dare say I speak like an
ignoramus--to get on the staff of some other paper with less--less
objectionable principles? You must be in great request. Only to-day
Mr. Drake was saying what a wonderful faculty you had for<pb
n="158     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> getting up subjects at short notice, and
writing brilliantly upon them.'</p>
<p>Faustina's lip assumes that ferocious curl so frequent in the pages
of novels, so rare in real life, but on this occasion really on
view.</p>
<p>'It is very good of him to allow me even that trifling merit!'</p></div>

<pb n="[159]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="8"><head>CHAPTER VIII.</head>
<p>THE hatchet is buried, though to a very nice observer a bit of its
handle may still be seen protruding from the ground. But to the
ordinary eye there would seem to be no alteration in the relation of
the friends as they go together, on the following day, to an
'advanced' tea-party.</p>
<p>They have been wise enough to avoid a reconciliation--a thing which
always leaves so much larger a cicatrice than the smartest
quarrel.</p>
<p>Althea has had a sleepless night; but by morning the deity which
had seemed to be sprawling as hopelessly as Dagon has been<pb
n="160     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> respectfully lifted to its pedestal
again. That pedestal is not quite so high a one as before; but if the
idol's feet have been shown to be clay, its head is not less
undoubtedly pure gold.</p>
<p>If there have been stains revealed upon Faustina's falchion, she is
none the less a valiant fighter in the host of righteousness and
pity. Such stout combatants have in all ages of the world not been
over-nice as to the quality of the weapons that came to their
hands. If these ingenious reasonings have not quite cured the gashed
wound of overnight, they have at least changed its pain from an
intolerably sharp to a quite supportably dull one.</p>
<p>The tea-party--a weekly one--is held at a club lately started with
the object of aiding needy young women writers of reforming views; and
if to this latter class have been added as members a few fine ladies,
who find its incendiary principles and risky discussions<pb
n="161     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> titillating, the original element still
predominates.</p>
<p>It is Miss Vane's first visit, and, as they have arrived rather
late, the room is crowded, and the din of 'advanced' tongues
stunning. Faustina is at once absorbed into a vortex of female
intimates, after presenting her friend to the president and secretary
of the institution, who in turn introduce her with bated breath to
various celebrities of whom she has never heard--gods of a little
esoteric clique, whose godhood seldom reaches the large inferior outer
world.</p>
<p>She is ushered with peculiar pomp into the acquaintance of one
whose name she is vaguely conscious of having seen in publishers'
advertising columns. In a happy flash it dawns upon her that it was in
connection with a volume of one of the now frequent 'Series.'</p>
<p>They talk happily for a few moments, when an allusion to her 'work'
on the part<pb n="162     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> of the lioness emboldens
Althea to hazard the remark that she believes the lady has not essayed
fiction.</p>
<p>'I have written one novel.'</p>
<p>'Oh, indeed! I--I did not know. I have not been fortunate enough to
meet with it.'</p>
<p>'And yet it went through three editions!'--not quite suavely.</p>
<p>'I--I have not time to read many novels; and'--determined to keep
to sure ground--'I always think of you as a biographer.'</p>
<p>'A <hi rend="italic">biographer?</hi>'--with raised eyebrows.</p>
<p>'Yes'--with rising misgivings, and a sincere desire to be 'over the
border and awa'.' 'Did not you write the "Life of Anna Maria Schumann"
in the "Gifted Women's Series"?'</p>
<p>'Yes, I wrote that.'</p>
<p>'And'--encouraged by this ray of success--'and the "Sappho"?'</p>
<p>'No, certainly not!'--rather shortly. 'Mme. --- wrote
that.'</p><pb n="163     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>A baffled pause.</p>
<p>'How nice-looking that tall young lady is!'--indicating one in the
near distance, and with a sudden plunge into what seems a safe
subject. But it, too, has its pitfalls.</p>
<p>'Yes; you know, of course, who she is?'</p>
<p>'I am sorry to say that I do not.'</p>
<p>'She is Mrs. Algernon Smithers.'</p>
<p>'Oh!'--rather blankly.</p>
<p>'You probably only know her by her pseudonym  "Hellas?' As the
listener's face remains distressfully unenlightened: 'You have, of
course, in common with the whole of the cultured world, enjoyed her
"Ode to Priapus"? It is more Greek than anything since
Theocritus.'</p>
<p>'I am afraid'--now sore ashamed--'that I am very ignorant of the
new poets.'</p>
<p>'<hhi rend="italic">New!</hhi> "Hellas" has been writing for ten
years. She and I began simultaneously.'</p>
<p>The mischief is out! The lady is a <hi
rend="italic">poet</hi>. This is only one of miany blunders and<pb
n="164     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> disasters. They multiply so much upon Miss
Vane's head that she looks round at last with a despairing impulse of
flight. But the wedging is too close for anything but a very slow
progress towards the door, and Faustina too unattainably distant and
surrounded for any looks of distress to reach her.</p>
<p>Althea's eyes rove helplessly over the unknown crowd--both over
those ladies whose gallant feathers and careful red heads show them to
be mere butterfly spectators of the fray, and those others whose
wildly cropped grizzled hair and super-manly coats and waistcoats
point them out as the nucleus and core--the female 'Old Guard,' as it
were--of the army of advance.</p>
<p>It is with a feeling of strong surprise that she presently
recognizes among--or, rather, soaring above--the surge of heads the
face of the girl whom she had yesterday met at luncheon at Clare's,
and against poisoning<pb n="165     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> whose mind with
her own megrims Edward had so cruelly warned her.</p>
<p>What on earth can she be doing in this galley? And what would
Edward's feelings be if he could see her here?</p>
<p>She has scarcely time for the thought, before Miss Delafield,
having worked her way to her with that ease which having your head and
shoulders above the human mass which is impeding your lower half
gives, stands beside her, holding out an obviously delighted hand.</p>
<p>'Oh, Miss Vane, I am <hi rend="italic">so</hi> glad to meet you here! I hoped that I
perhaps might. I forgot to ask you for your address yesterday, and I
could not persuade Mr. Vane to give it me; he turned the subject off
every time I mentioned it.'</p>
<p>'Did he?'</p>
<p>'But I felt I must see you again, to tell you--please do not think
me impertinent--how ardently I admire--and <hi rend="
italic">envy</hi> you.'</p><pb n="166     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'It is very good of you to say so; but for what?'</p>
<p>'Oh, surely you must know for what! For doing such a grand
thing. Throwing over everything--running against everybody
to--to--'</p>
<p>The action described sounds so very much more like that of an
animal not generally admired--a bull in a china-shop--than anything
else, that Althea cannot forbear a vexed smile.</p>
<p>'I hope I have not quite done that.'</p>
<p>'Oh, but I admire you so much for it! I know that I express myself
badly; but I think it such a splendid thing to let no obstacle stop
you in your path to what you think right. The moment that one begins
to try to do right--the highest right, I mean--how many, many
obstacles one finds!'</p>
<p>She says it with a pensive note as of personal experience, and
Althea knows that she is alluding to the good-natured nobleman<pb
n="167     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> and noblewoman who have had the honour of
endowing the world with so many feet of beauty and aspiration. She
looks up with silent misgiving at the pretty face in the seven-guinea
hat above her--so pretty, so much in earnest, and so far from
wise.</p>
<p>'My mother does not know that I am here to-day. I persuaded Lady
Treadwin to bring me; she has just become a member. But do not let us
waste time talking of me; I want you to tell me about yourself. You
live, do not you, with a friend, a high-minded friend, who has thrown
over everything, too? Is she here? Would you mind presenting me to
her?'</p>
<p>Again a thought of Edward, a thought even more rueful than amused,
darts across his sister's mind. Is this the young lady of whom he
confidently predicated that she would not be likely to have much in
common with <hi rend="italic">her</hi>, Althea? But blood is thicker than water--possibly at
this time yesterday it<pb n="168     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> would not have
been; she will not be the channel of introduction.</p>
<p>'I am afraid it would not be possible to get hold of her just
now.'</p>
<p>'No, oh no! I see that it would not; and I hope I shall have many
other opportunities; and, after all, it is you who are--whom I--I
thought that perhaps you would allow me to call upon you. One ought
not to be content with admiring people like you; one ought to try to
imitate them. But it is difficult--so difficult to break away! I
thought you would perhaps tell me how you did it--how you began?'</p>
<p>Instead of complying, Miss Vane looks back and up at her
interlocutor with an expression that might be described without much
exaggeration as <hi rend="italic">aghast</hi>.</p>
<p>'I do not think that our cases are alike enough to make it of much
use for me to do that. My father's death--the breaking up of my
home--'</p><pb n="169     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Ah yes; that, of course, simplified matters for you.'</p>
<p>She says it in a tone of pensive envy, and once again that sense of
aghastness rolls over the elder girl. The devotion of Lord and Lady
Lanington to their beautiful ewe lamb is proverbial; and that she
should be now calmly alluding to them merely as disagreeable obstacles
in her path to truth and glory makes Althea feel as if she herself
had set rolling a boulder down a precipice on their innocent heads, as
they sit hand in hand--they have always been a model pair--at the
hill-foot.</p>
<p>It is possible that her features express something of her
consternation, for the voice of her votary sounds less assured in her
next speech.</p>
<p>'But you had difficulties to contend with? Please do not think me
impertinent, but I was told that you had had a great deal to go
through.'</p><pb n="170     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Miss Vane is spared the embarrassment of having to answer this
question by the fact that at this point the secretary of the club
brings up another lady to present to her, a lady too young, as she
with a relieved feeling sees, to have as yet achieved any great
renown, and about whom, therefore, she need not fear to repeat her
distressing blunder of half an hour ago. She does not catch this new
acquaintance's name, and thinks it safest to tell her so.</p>
<p>'Oh, my name would not convey anything to you. I do a great deal of
anonymous work journalizing. There is a great field for women in
journalism; it is where general information tells.'</p>
<p>Althea is turning over in her mind whether this statement does not
contain an unintended implication that accuracy is not the forte of
the now confessedly superior sex, when the young lady adds: 'My
mother's name will be no doubt<pb n="171     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> familiar
to you, though mine is not--Beachy Head.'</p>
<p>An overpowering sense of crass ignorance whelms Althea, and must be
conveyed by her face, for, as in the case of the poetess, the other's
look of confident expectation pales.</p>
<p>'She writes under that sobriquet. She thought that it conveyed her
position in the world of speculative thought.'</p>
<p>Althea looks wildly round, and her eye alights on Miss Delafield,
still hovering anxiously near. But to take refuge with her would be to
fall out of the frying-pan into the fire. It is with genuine relief
that she sees Faustina masterfully ploughing a path towards her
through the female sea. She nods familiarly to the young journalist,
but her words are for Althea.</p>
<p>'I am afraid I must take you away; it is later than I thought.' In
a lower tone: 'You look fagged, darling. Is it so?'</p><pb n="172
DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Though the tone is low, the speech is overheard by Miss Delafield,
and its tenderness reveals the speaker. A glance of quickened
excitement passes over her face, and she draws a step nearer. Faustina
looks back at her, and then both half turn towards Althea, plainly
asking an introduction. But the thought of Ned is strong in his
sister's mind, and she makes as though she sees not.</p>
<p>'Let us come. I am quite, quite ready.'</p>
<p>Her disappointed votary does not get even a parting hand-shake from
her. As they stand at the street-corner, waiting to pick out their red
Hammersmith bus from the endless multicoloured file, Faustina
asks:</p>
<p>'Who was your pretty May-pole?'</p>
<p>'Miss Delafield.'</p>
<p>'A bit of the old life, I suppose?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'But perhaps with aspirations after something better?'</p><pb
n="173     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'If she has, they are not of a kind that can ever be of the
smallest use.'</p>
<p>'H'm!'</p>
<p>'She is not in the least of our sort.'</p>
<p>'Our! How sweet of you, love,' to bracket us together! But, as I
have often told you, it is all grist that comes to my mill. And she
looks to belong to the very class--the aristocratic "iced slugs"--that
I want to get hold of. I wish I had asked you to introduce me to
her.'</p>
<p>'Do you?'</p>
<p>'But I dare say I shall have other opportunities.'</p>
<p>'I dare say.'</p>
<p>They reach home in silence, and Althea turns into her meagre
bedroom. There is a sense of fatigue, of arrested enthusiasm, upon
her, and it is in a not very brisk voice that she answers Faustina's
knock and request to enter, made not five minutes after they have
parted. She comes in with sparkling eyes<pb n="174     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> and a paper in her hand. Althea's eyes fall on the name of
the journal.</p>
<p>'Since when have you became a reader of the <hi
rend="italic">Morning Post?</hi>'</p>
<p>'Since when indeed? But I had a special reason for buying it. Your
aunt Lavinia gives one of her big political parties on Wednesday.'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>The word sounds indifferent, but Miss Vane's heart in uttering it
seems to have slipped under the soles of her feet. Faustina has sat
down on the bed beside her friend--in the flat there is no vulgar
superfluity of chairs--and taken her hand with an air of almost
solemnity.</p>
<p>'My heart's dear one, you wounded me last night by an
implication--perhaps a just one--that I am not single-minded in my
devotion to the cause of suffering humanity; that I allow motives of
personal interest to sway my conduct. Nay, do not be afraid'--<pb
n="175     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> as Althea makes a deprecating gesture; 'I
have no wish to reopen the subject, except to tell you that you have
now an opportunity of proving--what I never doubted--of how much purer
metal <hi rend="italic">you</hi> are made.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">How?</hi>'--very faintly.</p>
<p>'If you remember, on the first night of your being here, you asked
me how you could make yourself of most use, and I told you
socially. Do you recollect?'</p>
<p>'I recollect your saying so.'</p>
<p>'I said it because it was, and is, my firm conviction. That, then,
is where you could <hi rend="italic">really</hi> help.'</p>
<p>Althea moves restlessly.</p>
<p>'Have I been of no help, then, all these weeks?'</p>
<p>'Of course you have. Your sweet presence has been an untold
support; but as to the actual work you have done, hundreds of women
with not a tithe of your gifts, but with the wholesome habit of
labour, could<pb n="176     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> have done it better;
whereas in the direction and for the end I point out to you, you
would be unique.'</p>
<p>There is a most uncomfortable silence, and when at length it is
broken, it is not by Althea.</p>
<p>'If you feel that the test is a severer one than you can bear, I
will, of course, not urge you; only, dearest, if it is so, I would ask
you in future to be a little more lenient to other fallible
mortals.'</p>
<p>Neither the perfectly good-humoured tone in which this last clause
is spoken, nor the caress by which it is accompanied, takes, nor is,
perhaps, intended to take, the sting out of it, and Miss Vane
writhes.</p>
<p>'You are right; I have no business to preach to others, and yet
flinch when my own turn comes. No doubt it is not because there seems
to me something as unworthy and underhand in picking people's
confidence as their purse, but because it would be so<pb n="177
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> intensely disagreeable to myself, that I shrink. What
is it you want me to do?'</p>
<p>'My noble darling, I knew that you only needed to have it brought
home to you.'</p>
<p>'What do you want me to do?'</p>
<p>Faustina has the sense to see that her friend would rather that she
dropped her hand, and she does so, while the businesslike glitter
comes into her black eyes.</p>
<p>'You have heard me speak of the Child Insurance Bill?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'You know how keen I am to get up the facts about it?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'And how hard I have found it to do so?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'How impossible to approach the Home Secretary?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'Well, through you, I have now an opportunity of getting at
him. The Ministers are<pb n="178     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> sure to be at
your aunt's party, and he among them.'</p>
<p>'So will two hundred other people be.'</p>
<p>'I think you once told me that he was an old friend of yours?'</p>
<p>'Of my father's. My acquaintance with him is very slight.'</p>
<p>'But enough to justify your addressing him, I suppose?'</p>
<p>'I suppose so.'</p>
<p>A pause. Althea feels the net closing round her, but she makes one
more despairing effort to break through its meshes.</p>
<p>'My aunt is not in the least likely to send me a card.'</p>
<p>'And you could not get one through Clare--Mrs. Boteler, as I
suppose I ought to call her?'</p>
<p>'If she suspected my motive for asking it, certainly not.'</p>
<p>'Why need she suspect it? Why need anyone suspect it?'</p><pb
n="179     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Althea starts up and goes to the window, inhaling as much air as
the blank wall, three feet off, opposite, and the projection of their
own kitchen, thrusting itself forward at right angles, to still
further cut off any troublesome zephyrs, allow her to do. It is this
very underhandedness, what seems to her the social treachery of her
intended <hi rend="italic">r&#x00F4;le</hi>, which makes it so hard a
mouthful to swallow.</p>
