<p>'It was our own fault. We ought to have had the big hall. Next
time, if we invite "friends," we must have the big hall.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Next time?</hi> Will there ever be a next time? Do you mean to say
that, after the way in which we have treated you to-night, you will
ever venture among us again?'</p><pb n="269     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>They have reached the station, and by one consent pause facing each
other, ere entering it. Her gay excitement gives way to a touched
gravity.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Ever venture among you again!</hi>' she says, repeating his words. 'Do
you know that sometimes--often of late--it has struck me that, if it
were not for Faustina and all I owe her, I should like to come among
you for good!'</p></div>

<pb n="[270]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="13"><head>CHAPTER XIII.</head>
<p>IT is in a very much brighter mood than she has for some time
enjoyed that Althea, on the following morning, is walking through the
Park on an errand for Faustina. She does not pry too nicely into the
component parts of her good spirits, though, if the question were
pressed, she could give a very handsome and creditable account of
them.</p>
<p>But there is no use to seek officiously an explanation of her
unwonted light-heartedness; and she enjoys it, as she does the
flower-beds between Stanhope and Grosvenor Gates, which are just
beginning to develop the intricate beauty of their bedded-out pat<pb
n="271     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>terns, and console the 'fond gazer' for
the departed hyacinths and tulips.</p>
<p>She is quickening her pace as she nears the Marble Arch, and the
floral temptations to linger lessen, when she is aware that one of the
carriages rolling in the same direction as herself is pulling up at
the rails alongside of her.</p>
<p>She has got into the habit of not looking at the occupants of any
of the barouches and victorias that pass or meet her, in order to
avoid the tiresomeness of recognition by some of the former
acquaintances from whom her present course of life has separated her;
but a glance at the large smart vehicle which has evidently stopped
<hi rend="italic">&#x00E0; son intention</hi> is now unavoidable, and
in its solitary occupant she at once recognizes the mother of
Cressida Delafield.</p>
<p>Despite her real innocence of any sin against Lady Lanington's
peace, she is the one of her late society whom she would least<pb
n="272     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> soon have come across; and it is with a
sinking heart that, in obedience to the sound of her own name, she now
stays her steps.</p>
<p>'Oh, Miss Vane, I am going in your direction.'</p>
<p>An earnest desire to avoid the 'lift' so obviously about to be
offered is 'writ so large' on Althea's face that the person who has
addressed her adds hastily:</p>
<p>'Indeed, in whatever direction you are going--it is all one to
me--may I take you?'</p>
<p>To so limitless an invitation refusal is out of the question, and
the girl--since the blessed 'I had rather not,' which would rescue us
all from so many unpleasant pleasures, is relegated to Utopia--with a
civil answer and leaden heels, walks on the few necessary steps to the
next opening in the railings, and in another moment the two ladies are
seated side by side, and the vehicle, after Althea has given an
address of which<pb n="273     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> the coachman feels, or
feigns, a dignified ignorance, rolls on again.</p>
<p>'I wanted so much to meet you,' is the elder's opening.</p>
<p>The younger is so very far from echoing this wish that a smile,
which may pass at a pinch for one of acquiescence, is the 'nearest
thing she can do' to it.</p>
<p>'I thought of writing to you.'</p>
<p>'Did you?'</p>
<p>'But I could not help feeling that a personal appeal would be
better.'</p>
<p>'An--an appeal?'</p>
<p>'Only I did not know how to manage it. You are never to be seen
anywhere about now, and Cressida has positively refused to give me
your address.'</p>
<p>'Indeed!'</p>
<p>'I was so fond of your father, and I always used to like you so
much.'</p>
<p>The exceeding discomfort of Althea's mind in her present situation
is here crossed by<pb n="274     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> the bitter
reflection that whoever now speaks of a liking for her puts it into
the past tense.</p>
<p>'It--it was very good of you !' she stammers baldly.</p>
<p>'How little I thought in those days--I mean during your dear
father's lifetime--that it would be <hi rend="italic">your</hi> hand which would deal me such
a blow!'</p>
<p>'I do not know--you must please explain--what you are alluding
to.'</p>
<p>Although vaguely prepared for something disagreeable, a look of
startled dismay has come into the girl's face; but her speech has a
truthful dignity that her companion is too much agitated and
preoccupied to perceive.</p>
<p>'Oh, do not let us have any fencing!' she cries impatiently; 'we
both know what we mean--why should we pretend that we do not?'</p>
<p>'I must emphatically answer that I do not know.'</p>
<p>The rejoinder, though made very gently<pb n="274     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> and civilly, seems to drive the hearer over the limits,
already reached, of self-control.</p>
<p>'Oh, Miss Vane, is it possible that you are going to add to the
injury of having robbed me of my daughter the insult of denying
it?'</p>
<p>The words are rude even to violence; but they produce no sense of
resentment in Althea's breast. It is with a compassion largely
streaked with fellow-feeling that she looks at the twitched, flushed
features of the usually good-natured, well-bred woman beside her.</p>
<p>'You are mistaken. I am truly sorry that you imagine anyone has
robbed you of your daughter; but I assure you it is not I.'</p>
<p>'No doubt you do not call it robbing'--in a perfectly unconvinced
and still more exasperated key--'but that is a mere quibble; you <hi rend="italic">did</hi>
rob me of her by introducing her to that horrible, horrible woman
who--'</p>
<p>'If you are alluding to Miss Bateson, I<pb n="276     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> must again repeat that I did not introduce them to each
other; I do not even know who did.'</p>
<p>'But for you she would never have known her--never have wished to
know her! It was your example--your <hi rend="italic">fatal</hi> example--'</p>
<p>Althea has turned very pale. There are limits even to her
patience.</p>
<p>'Will you mind setting me down?' she says in a low voice. 'I do not
see that any purpose is served by my staying with you; you do not
believe a word I say.'</p>
<p>The request brings Lady Lanington back in some measure to a
recollection of the claims of good manners, forgotten as they always
are when the elemental emotions have us in their clutch.</p>
<p>'Oh, pray do not go! I have so much more that I want to say to
you. I had no intention of being rude; the words escaped me. I really
scarcely know what I am doing or saying!'</p><pb n="277     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Her agitation is so painfully obvious, and the passion that
dictates it has so clearly broken down all the dykes of good-breeding
and habit, that Althea's short-lived wrath dies out.</p>
<p>'I would not mind what kind of things you said to me,' she rejoins
gently, 'if it did you any good; but indeed I think you are making
yourself unnecessarily miserable. As far as I am aware, Miss Delafield
and the person whose influence you so much dread for her never now
meet except in the most casual passing way.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Never now meet!</hi>' repeats the other, in a tone of indignant
incredulity; and the eyes which, at the softness of the girl's
answer, had begun to twinkle behind her pince-nez with tears, now
blaze again with angry distrust.</p>
<p>Althea's heart sinks, but she replies steadily: 'Never, upon my
honour, to my know<pb n="278     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>ledge, except in the
way I have mentioned.'</p>
<p>Then, as her companion continues to glare at her with ireful
disbelief, she adds: 'Miss Delafield spent two nights at our flat
upon Miss Bateson's invitation, but that was weeks ago; and since
then--'</p>
<p>'Since then you are under the impression that they have never
met?'</p>
<p>'I have never heard of their having done so.'</p>
<p>The perfect steadiness with which Althea sustains the mother's
angry scrutiny seems at length to convince the latter of the truth of
her asseverations, for she says, in a changed key:</p>
<p>'If that is your belief, I can only tell you that they have been
keeping you in the dark.'</p>
<p>'What do you mean?'</p>
<p>'What do I mean? I mean that, so far from the intimacy between my
daughter and that--that person being at an end, as you<pb n="279
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> seem to imagine, not a day--scarcely a day passes
without their spending hours of it together. In Cressida it has become
a madness, a frenzy; in the other--well, as she is your friend, I will
not qualify it; but if she were not, I should say that it is an
iniquitous case of child-stealing!'</p>
<p>While Lady Lanington, with growing excitement, has been running up
the gamut of her woes, the knowledge has come coldly home to Althea
that she had had an instinctive foreboding of what is now being told
her all along. None the less does the certainty of her supersession,
and of the smashing of her ideal, strike her dumb and
unprotesting.</p>
<p>'She used to be such a dear, affectionate child--never very
strong-minded, but so loving and nice'--this very falteringly--'and
now-- Oh, tell him to drive on anywhere--round the
Park--anywhere'--this to the footman, who has got down to ask<pb
n="280     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> for minuter directions as to Althea's
obscure destination. 'And <hi rend="italic">now!</hi> She is to come of age next
month. Unfortunately, she is quite independent of us pecuniarily, as
she inherits from an uncle; and last night she told us, her father and
me--oh, I can scarcely bear to repeat it--'</p>
<p>'What did she tell you?'</p>
<p>'I can hardly believe it even now; it seems incredible!'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'If anyone had prophesied it to me six months ago, I should have
laughed in their face.'</p>
<p>'But you have not yet told me what it is.'</p>
<p>'She told us--and oh, Miss Vane, to think that we should owe it
(indirectly, at all events) to <hi rend="italic">you!</hi>--that she was weary of the idle,
senseless, soul-numbing existence she was compelled by us to lead, and
that since, while she remained with us, all her best energies were
paralyzed, and she was prevented from<pb n="281     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
following out the high ends for which she was created (I am quoting
the poor child verbally), it would be best for us to part.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="smallcaps">Part!</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes, part. And when we found words to remonstrate with her--at
first, as you may imagine, we were paralyzed with grief and
astonishment--she quoted you as a triumphant instance of a girl who
had cut herself adrift from family ties for conscience' sake.'</p>
<p>The 'triumphant instance' does not much justify the adjective
assigned to her, as she sits wide-eyed in wretched listening. Among
the chaos of painful feelings in which Lady Lanington's words are
making her welter, one has risen prominently to the surface. It
dictates the speech which comes--half hurry, half lag--across her
lips.</p>
<p>'If she is going to part from you and Lord Lanington, whom does she
mean to join? She will not live alone, I suppose?'</p><pb n="282
DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'She absolutely refused to answer that question when it was put to
her; but I can guess--I can guess! I thought that you were her
accomplice; but I begin to believe--I quite believe--that you are
not.'</p>
<p>To a proposition so monstrous as that she has been wielding the axe
to cut off her own head, Althea is incapable of a rejoinder.</p>
<p>'But that is not the worst--not nearly the worst! Oh, I hardly know
how to tell you! putting it into words seems to make it worse. Do you
know--because, if you do, it will spare me the shame of telling you;
but I see by your face that you do not--do you know the kind of work
that my poor insane child is going to devote herself to?--she told us
so to our faces!'</p>
<p>'What work?'</p>
<p>'I would not have believed it on any less evidence--at her age,
with her appearance--'</p><pb n="283     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Oh, what--<hi rend="smallcaps">What?</hi> Why do not you tell
me?'</p>
<p>Althea has unconsciously grasped the arm of her companion that is
nearest to her, and her strenuous pressure seems to squeeze out the
difficult answer.</p>
<p>'She is going to devote her life'--with a voice sunk almost beyond
the audible, and an apprehensive glance at the servants' backs--to <hi
rend="italic">rescue work!</hi> Do you understand? At her age, and
with her appearance, she is going out into the Haymarket at night
among those degraded creatures--' She breaks off, adding in another
key: 'You are not going to <hi rend="italic">faint?</hi>'</p>
<p>'No, no; I never fainted in my life. Go on.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Go on!</hi>' repeats the other in a tone of the bitterest indignation. 'Is not that enough?
What more would you have?'</p>
<p>Apparently it is quite enough for the auditor, whose blanched
rigidity of look<pb n="284     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> calls forth a
repetition of Lady Lanington's just-expressed fear.</p>
<p>'I believe you <hi rend="italic">are</hi> going to faint.'</p>
<p>'No, no!'</p>
<p>'Can't you help me? Can't you do something to prevent such a crime,
such an <hi rend="italic">outrage?</hi>  You must have influence with
this woman, since you gave up everything--quarrelled with your whole
family--for her sake.'</p>
<p>'I have never quarrelled with my whole family'--faintly.</p>
<p>'Oh, what does it matter what you call it? I will call it by what
name you like; but you cannot deny that there is entire separation
between you and them, and that she is the cause. In return, you must
have some influence with her; you cannot deny that you have influence
with her, if only you would use it.'</p>
<p>The mother's tone has changed from a key of bitterest upbraiding to
one of almost<pb n="285     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> abject entreaty, and to
emphasize her request she wrings the girl's fingers with an even
tighter grip than Althea, in the height of her excitement, had used a
few moments ago towards herself.</p>
<p>Althea almost laughs. <hi rend="smallcaps">Her influence!</hi> But
even now she cannot bear to admit to a third person the only
half-realized depth of her own fall.</p>
<p>'What would you have me do?'</p>
<p>'Do! Why, go to her, beg her, entreat her, command her--you know
what arguments have most hold on such a--such a--to let my child go!
She will be able to find plenty more victims to infect with her
pestilent opinions! Is not it enough for her to have been the ruin of
<hi rend="italic">you?</hi>'</p>
<p>Althea gives a horrified start.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Ruin!</hi> How dare you apply such a word to
me?'</p>
<p>But the mother is off again on the track of her own woe, and does
not seem to hear her.</p><pb n="286     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You cannot refuse me this reparation, such a poor one as it is,
for the horrible wrong you have done me. After all, it is you that
have done it--indirectly, at least. I should have had my child still
with me if she had not learnt from you, from your example, to laugh at
all constituted authorities, at religion, at decency, at whatever she
had been taught to respect.'</p>
<p>Lady Lanington pauses, not, certainly, because she has exhausted
her armoury of vituperation, but arrested by the deathly whiteness
beside her, and adds: 'Oh, I do not know what I am saying. I can keep
no measure, can think of nothing but--'</p>
<p>Under the storm of obloquy that has hailed upon her, Althea has put
her hand to her head, as if some stone had hit her; but she now
straightens her limp back, and sits up.</p>
<p>'I must again ask you to let me get out; indeed, I must insist upon
it. It is necessary<pb n="287     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> for me to go home
at once, and inquire into the truth of the charges you have been
bringing against my--my <hi rend="italic">friend</hi>; to find out
how much of mistake and misapprehension there is in them.'</p>
<p>'And if you find that they are <hi rend="italic">true</hi>--<hi
rend="smallcaps">true</hi>--true as Gospel?'</p>
<p>Again the slight hand goes up to the brow that still smarts from
its lapidation.</p>
<p>'I cannot--<hi rend="italic">cannot</hi> believe it.'</p>
<p>'But <hi rend="italic">if</hi>--<hi rend="smallcaps">if</hi>--'</p>
<p>'I will not face such an <hi rend="italic">if</hi>.'</p></div>

<pb n="[288]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="14"><head>CHAPTER XIV.</head>
<p>To her coachman's disgust, Lady Lanington insists upon driving
Althea to the portal of her flat, in the feverish hope that the
interview between the latter and Miss Bateson may be thereby
hastened. During the drive the younger woman scarcely speaks, save to
put a decided veto upon the elder's proposal that she shall await the
result outside, and to give, on leaving her, a mechanical assent to
the distracted prayer that she will not keep her in suspense one
second longer than is necessary.</p>
<p>It is with a foggy sense of relief at being alone that Althea
speeds up the dirty stairs,<pb n="289     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> and with a
mixed and also foggy feeling of eagerness to face the worst and desire
to shove the crucial moment a little farther off that she presses her
door-bell. It is the first of these aspirations which is destined to
be gratified, as it is Faustina herself who opens.</p>
<p>One glance at Miss Bateson's face shows her house-fellow that it
was not she who was expected, though to a stranger the ready
ejaculation, 'Back already, darling! Well, you <hi rend="italic">are</hi> an ideal
messenger!' would seem to hold even more rapture than
astonishment. The bitterness of the intuition, which shows Miss Vane
that she has been hoodwinked into being got out of the way to clear
the stage for her supplanter, gives her the impetus necessary for an
instant rush upon the fray.</p>
<p>'I have not been to Rodney Street.'</p>
<p>'No? Then, why are you back?'</p>
<p>Faustina is still fondly smiling, but in her tone there is the
slight tang of displeasure<pb n="290     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> of a General
whose aide-de-camp has gratuitously disobeyed him.</p>
<p>'Because I met Lady Lanington.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Because you met Lady Lanington!</hi> That sounds
rather a <hi rend="italic">non sequitur</hi>.'</p>
<p>'She told me something which made it necessary that I should return
home at once.'</p>
<p>'You are dealing in riddles, dearest. If it is quite convenient to
you, I should like to know what you are talking about.'</p>
<p>The tone is playful, and might possibly have deceived Althea into a
belief that her antagonist is ignorant of the coming thrust, had not
she detected an instantaneous flash of consciousness in the eyes--eyes
at once, and in a second, on their guard again.</p>
<p>They are in the drawing-room by now; and if other indications of a
rising storm were wanting, the care with which Althea closes the
door--a door generally left to bang, to jar, or to gape, according to
its own wild will--would suffice as a warning.</p><pb n="291     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'She told me facts--a fact--about her daughter which I refused to
believe.'</p>
<p>'Indeed! That was not very polite of you.'</p>
<p>'Faustina, were those facts--was that fact true?'</p>
<p>'As I have not any R&#x00F6;ntgen rays to turn upon your mind, I
must respectfully repeat that I am in the dark as to what you are
alluding to.'</p>
<p>'It is useless to try and put me off with a jest. Was it--is it
true?'</p>
<p>'Was <hi rend="italic">what</hi>--is what true?'</p>
<p>The waxing pressure of the one speaker, and the waning gaiety of
the other, though the latter is obviously anxious to cling as long as
possible to her light tone, reveal that the stress of the storm is
nigh.</p>
<p>'I was told by Lady Lanington that you have contracted an intimacy
with  her daughter.'</p>
<p>'Well?'</p><pb n="292     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Faustina has sat down. In a quarrel the sitter has always an
advantage over the stander, as evidencing a greater self-control; and
her 'Well?' is uttered with a cold and slightly contemptuous patience,
which makes the indictment fall flat even upon the indicter's ear.</p>
<p>'That you have been meeting her secretly--'</p>
<p>'There was no secrecy about it.'</p>
<p>'That you have been having daily--almost daily meetings with her
all through the time during which you conveyed--implied to me that you
have had no intercourse with her.'</p>
<p>'I never conveyed or implied anything about the subject to
you.'</p>
<p>Once again there is a controlled contempt in the unhesitating
answer, which, making the less-skilled combatant feel the apparent
paltriness of her preliminary accusations, hurries her to the
gist.</p><pb n="293     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'That you have been setting her against her parents.'</p>
<p>'Against such parents, it was the kindest thing I could do--the
greatest service I could render her.'</p>
<p>'That you have been inducing her to embrace--go in for a line of
work which, though no doubt a great and necessary one when undertaken
by the proper people, is grossly, indecently unsuitable for a girl of
her age, character, and appearance!'</p>
<p>In the first part of this sentence there is an attempt at judicial
calm, but the latter half comes, contrary to its utterer's intention,
in intemperate, scarlet hurry.</p>
<p>'Are you alluding to her wish to devote herself to "rescue"
work?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>Faustina heaves a sigh--the kind of sigh which any and all of the
world's great teachers and creed-founders may have uttered, when the
inability of their disciples to understand<pb n="294     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> their lessons was brought home to them--a sigh of
impatient patience.</p>
<p>'I suppose I had better answer your accusations in the order in
which you have brought them. I <hi rend="italic">have</hi> been seeing
a good deal of Cressida Delafield.'</p>
<p>She pauses, as if to give her companion time for a rejoinder, but
none comes, so she goes on:</p>
<p>'The secrecy with which you twit me consists in my not having
thought it necessary to impart to you a fact whose true bearing my
knowledge of your character and disposition taught me you would be
unable to comprehend.'</p>
<p>Althea brushes a hand quickly across her eyes, not because a tear
is within miles of them, but because of the mist of delusion which the
tone of calm and lenient explanation with which Miss Bateson is
uttering her defence is calculated to draw over them.</p>
<p>'As for the rescue work which I have<pb n="295     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> persuaded her to take up--I do not for a moment deny that
it was my suggestion, which at the first hint she seized with joyful
alacrity--my defence--if defence is needed, which I am far from
admitting--is that, with me, the Cause always goes before the
individual. I look upon the persons whom I am able to influence
primarily as its instruments, and only very secondarily in their
relation to myself or to themselves.'</p>
<p>She shuts her lips, as if the subject were ended; and with another
sigh--of relief this time--leans carelessly back in her chair.</p>
<p>For a moment Althea clutches her temples with both hands; then she
speaks:</p>
<p>'I do not think you have been very successful in your choice of an
instrument this time.'</p>
<p>'No? I cannot agree with you. She has been very useful to me
already.'</p>
<p>'Useful! In what way?'</p>
<p>'By her social gifts she has succeeded in obtaining for me from the
proper sources.<pb n="296     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> that information about
the Child Insurance Bill which, as you may remember, you were rather
unsuccessful in getting.'</p>
<p>The shaft tells. A quiver of pain passes over Althea's face.</p>
<p>'Not that I blame you,' returns the other dispassionately. 'I quite
believe that you did your best.'</p>
<p>'I ought never to have attempted it.'</p>
<p>'So the result proved; but you must remember how much and often you
importuned me to put you to whatever branch of work I thought you best
fitted for.'</p>
<p>The very slight, but perceptible, flavour of contempt which seasons
this speech conveys to Althea how little adapted for any labour,
worthy of the name, her quondam friend regards her. It has the effect
of a whiplash curling and tingling round her shoulders.</p>
<p>'We are wandering from the point,' she says, in a high, strained
voice. 'It is no question now of me and my insufficiency, but<pb
n="297     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> of whether you are justified in kidnapping
a foolish young girl from her home, and setting her to an employment
of which it is always doubtful whether the good can predominate over
the evil, but which in her case--in her circumstances--would be a
disgrace--an outrage!'</p>
<p>The speaker stops, white and shaking; and there is a slight
answering alteration in Miss Bateson's steady complexion and composed
voice, when, after a moment's interval, to get herself well in hand,
she replies:</p>
<p>'I deny, absolutely and entirely, the right of you or of anyone
else to challenge my actions. I am my own judge and censor; to myself
I stand or fall. But in deference to the intimacy of the relations
that have subsisted between us, I am willing to give you as a favour
that explanation which I refuse you as a due.' She pauses, and then
adds dryly: 'Whether you will enjoy hearing it is another
question.'</p><pb n="298     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Go on.'</p>
<p>'I spared you the knowledge of my intercourse with Cressida
Delafield, not because I had any motive for concealment, but out of
tenderness to you--out of consideration for a weakness which from the
first I divined to exist in your character, but which until lately I
hoped might remain latent. You must know that I am alluding to that
tendency towards jealousy which I have always thought somewhat
unworthy of you.'</p>
<p>Only a quickened drawing of the listener's already short-drawn
breath as answer, so she goes on:</p>
<p>'As to your indictment of "kidnapping a foolish young girl,"
well'--with a shrug--'folly is a relative term. In some lights many of
us do not appear particularly wise'--a stung start shows that the
hearer has made the personal application intended--'and if to "kidnap"
is to do for her what I did for you--that is, to give her the
impetus<pb n="299     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> necessary for cutting herself
adrift from an ignoble <hi rend="italic">entourage</hi>--I not only admit, but I glory in,
the accusation.'</p>
<p>Still no rejoinder but that rapid breathing.</p>
<p>'With regard to the rescue work, which appears to be the head and
front of my offending, as I have already told you, with me the Cause
always goes before the individual. But even were it not so, even if I
were to allow personal feeling to outweigh abstract right, I should
still have no scruple in directing upon such a course one who, with no
prurient squeamishness, but with a noble alacrity, leapt at the first
suggestion to her post in the grandest crusade ever undertaken by
humanity.'</p>
<p>The voice is steady, the look quasi-inspired; the words are--except
for the side-hit at Althea's prurience--of much the same quality as
those which had often stirred her like a trumpet-call. Bitterly she
recognizes this, as they now fall in dead mockery on<pb n="300
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> her ear. It is a full minute before she regains
utterance.</p>
<p>'I am to understand, then, that you refuse to loose your prey?'</p>
<p>'You word it offensively. But, yes, I do refuse.'</p>
<p>'I know'--with an unsuccessful effort to imitate her companion's
sang-froid--'that you deny the authority of the Book that gives it,
but you must allow the justice of the prohibition to us to do evil
that good may come.'</p>
<p>'We start from different premises. I deny that I am doing
evil.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Not doing evil!</hi>'--the poor rag of judicial calm flung aside, and
with an outblazing of passionate expostulation which comes much more
naturally: 'Is not it doing evil to lay waste a happy home, to bring
desolation and ruin upon two good and innocent lives, even if the
question of the girl herself is waived?'</p><pb n="301     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'It cannot be waived; since it is the only one with which I have
any concern.'</p>
<p>'Does that mean that, in spite of what I have said, you are still
determined to carry out your scandalous and disgraceful plan?'</p>
<p>'You observed to me some little while ago that I was fond of
calling names. I think I might now retort the accusation.'</p>
<p>'I do not wish to call names, because I have not--there are not any
strong enough to characterize such an iniquity. But are you--you have
not answered me--still determined to stick to it?'</p>
<p>'What reason have you given me--bluster is not reasoning--for
abandoning it? But even if your powers of ratiocination were stronger
than they are, they would be powerless to move me from a course of
action of whose righteousness and desirability I am absolutely
convinced.'</p>
<p>'Then, all I can say is, that you will have<pb n="302     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> to choose between Cressida Delafield and me.'</p>
<p>The bolt which a month ago would have shattered the firmament, now
falls apparently innocuous; and so much still remains in Althea's mind
of the habit of belief in the eternity of their intimacy, that she
thinks Faustina cannot have grasped her proposition. She restates
it:</p>
<p>'If you adhere to your resolution, I shall be compelled to leave
you.'</p>
<p>'That is as you please.'</p>
<p>'At once.'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'And for ever.'</p>
<p>'We are certainly not very likely to resume our relations.'</p>
<p>There is a cool dryness, an indifferent common-sense, in this last
sentence which oversets the other's tottering balance.</p>
<p>'And this is what it has come to,' she says, clutching her own head
with both<pb n="303     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> hands, as if to assure
herself that it is still on her shoulders. 'After all your
protestations, this is what it has come to!'</p>
<p>'It is what you yourself have brought it to.'</p>
<p>With one of her grasping hands Althea hits herself on the
forehead.</p>
<p>'Oh, how blind I have been! How right my people were! How bitterly,
<hi rend="italic">bitterly</hi> disappointed, disillusioned, I am in you!'</p>
<p>'And do you think,' rejoins Faustina, in whom during the last
apostrophes signs of some emotion have become evident, and who, in
token of waning self-control, now rises from her careless sitting
posture to her feet--'and do you think, pray, that <hi
rend="italic">I</hi> have not been disappointed, disillusioned, in
you?'</p>
<p>To this agreeable inquiry Althea has no answer but dropped hands
and staring eyes.</p>
<p>'Do you think that as week by week, day by day, the paltriness of
your character unfolded itself; your inability to embrace a<pb
n="304     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> great design or to soar above petty
details--do you think, I say, that <hi rend="italic">my</hi> heart did not sink at the
thought of the clog with which I had fettered myself?'</p>
<p>Again receiving no audible reply, she sails on with spread
canvas.</p>
<p>'It is such as you, whose petulant feebleness, whose irritable
self-love, whose silly conventions and minute brain power, have
brought us where we are; have palliated, justified, explained man's
attitude to us.'</p>
<p>She pauses to take in a fresh supply of breath, and Althea's voice
makes itself just heard in a dreary whisper:</p>
<p>'That is enough! that is enough! I will go!'</p>
<p>'I shall make no attempt to stop you'--giving way with evident
relief to a long-pent burst of frank brutality--'but please to
remember that the breach comes from you; it would never have come from
me. Out of loyalty to my original idea of you, and as<pb n="305
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> a penance for my folly in crediting you with
excellences and aptitudes of which you are conspicuously destitute, I
should have gone on putting up with you, enduring even your
impertinent efforts to interfere with my best-laid and most deeply
considered schemes, and your contemptible willingness to be the
cat's-paw of John Drake.'</p>
<p>The storm of missiles which has been whistling round her head has
had the effect of rendering Althea dizzy and deaf, but this last
well-aimed flint stings her back into a cruelly full possession of her
senses:</p>
<p>'The--cat's-paw--of--John-Drake!'</p>
<p>'Yes, the cat's-paw of John Drake. I do not know why you should
have credited me with so much less keen sight than yourself; why you
should have supposed me ignorant of those frequent meetings with him
of late, which you have either <hi rend="italic">happened to forget, or not thought it
worth while to mention.</hi>'</p>
<p>A sort of dimness comes before the<pb n="306     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
hearer's vision. It is as if the blood of that flint-wound were
dripping into her eyes and blinding her.</p>
<p>'Do you think that I have not seen you, in spite of all I have told
you of the horror of men's lives, in spite of your hypocritical air of
repulsion--do you think that I have not seen you drifting into the
miserable old path, the wretched old attitude of inferiority and
appeal?  Has it ever struck you that, had I been cast in the same
mould as you, I, too, might have played at jealousy?'</p>
<p>The other's answer is nothing but a groping movement towards the
door, but Miss Bateson has not yet quite done with her.</p>
<p>'If I had not become aware of that headstrong self-opinion in you
which, coupled with your intellectual weakness, makes you so
impossible to deal with, I would have given you a friendly hint that,
since John Drake has a rather firmer hold upon his<pb n="307     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> convictions than you, your attentions to him are not
likely to lead to the only close which would seem a satisfactory one
to yourself and your highly respectable family.'</p>
<p>Then she lets her go, and the other, feeling first for the
drawing-room door, and then for the outer one, stumbles off down the
public stairs. Before she reaches the bottom of these, practical
common-sense has resumed its sway over Faustina's mind, and she calls
down the well of the staircase in much her ordinary voice:</p>
<p>'Your boxes shall be sent at once to whatever address you like to
give.'</p><milestone unit="section" rend="stars"/>
<p>In blind ignorance of the way she is taking, Althea walks
along--walks on and on. She is half conscious that she has reached
the Embankment by the pleasantness of the fresh air from the
river. Then she walks on and on again, half blind, half deaf, every
sense muffled like a knocker in a kid glove. She<pb n="308     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> has reached the end of the endless length of Grosvenor
Road before the sun, beating hotly on her head--she has left parasol
and gloves behind--and the urgent weariness of the knees that knock
together beneath her, bid her find some place of shelter.</p>
<p>The thought of the Aerated Bread Company passes foggily across her
mind--that beneficent institution which, during the last months, has
often provided her with a frugal luncheon or inexpensive tea. She has
to drag her tired limbs yet a little further before, in a street in
Westminster, the welcome letters 'A. B. C.' salute her eyes over a
shop-door. She enters, and sits down at one of the little round
marble-topped tables which chances to be vacant.</p>
<p>At first she is conscious only of a sense of bodily relief and
ease. It is not till instructed by the blank look of surprise on the
face of the waitress who comes to know her requirements--surprise at
the silence with which<pb n="309     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> Althea stares at
her--that the latter pulls herself together and orders a cup of coffee
and a wheat-cake.  When they come, she feels disappointed that, for
want of a preciser order, the cup is a small one. She drains it at a
draught: it does her the doubtful kindness of clearing her brain.</p>
<p>Leaning both elbows on the table, and taking her head in her two
hands, she reviews her situation. An earthquake has swallowed up her
home. At the memory of that so recent convulsion she shudders
strongly, then glances round, afraid lest she should have been
observed.</p>
<p>An earthquake has swallowed up her home! Where is she to find
another one?</p>
<p>But from this question, though she is aware in a woolly way that it
claims an immediate answer, mind and memory keep slipping back to the
exquisite humiliation of the past interview.</p>
<p>It is not the absoluteness and ignominy<pb n="310     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> of her failure to save Cressida, though at another time
that would have given her keen pain, which is crushing her. It is not
even the sight of the ignoble clay shards into which, under her eyes,
her reputed god of gold and silver has flown, shivered. It is in
those phrases into which Faustina had packed the poison of her final
sting that lies the secret of the girl's prostration.</p>
<p>She had carried her white maiden pennon so high; and now it lies
draggled and defiled in the filth of the public street.</p>
<p>'The cat's-paw of John Drake!' 'Attentions to him not likely to
lead to the only close!' etc. Horrible, horrible phrases! And can it
be that there is a grain of infinitely more horrible truth in them?
Has she paid him any attentions? Can this odious colouring be put upon
that intercourse which of late has formed the only solace of her
life? Her mind, having fastened upon this point, refuses to quit
it. Inquiries as to whether<pb n="311     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> no further
steps are possible for restoring to the Laningtons their strayed
child, and also as to where Althea is to house herself for the coming
night, drift across her brain, and remain indifferently
unanswered.</p>
<p>The one question that puts itself unceasingly as the only one
really worthy of response is, Has she paid attentions to John Drake?
The question is asked with shame-dropped head, and hands pentwise
shading burning eyes. It is not till a step stayed beside her singles
itself out from the coming and going feet in the restaurant that she
snatches herself upright, and sees that the object of her anguished
query is present to answer it if she will in person.</p></div>

<pb n="[312]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="15"><head>CHAPTER XV.</head>
<p>ALTHEA starts to her feet.</p>
<p>'What are you doing here?'</p>
<p>Drake looks at her in unfeigned surprise.</p>
<p>'I caught a glimpse of you through the door, and thought I would
come and ask whether you are any the worse for our excursion.'</p>
<p>'Our excursion?'</p>
<p>'Yes; have you already forgotten Canning Town?'</p>
<p>She does not answer; and, with growing alarm, he scans her more
narrowly.</p>
<p>'Has anything happened? Are you ill?'</p>
<p>No, I am not ill.'</p><pb n="313     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>There is something so indescribably frosty and distant in her voice
that he replaces the chair which he had begun to move from the table,
in order to sit down opposite her.</p>
<p>'You had rather be alone. I will go.'</p>
<p>His tone tells her what her own has been, and she makes a
frightened effort to be natural and normal.</p>
<p>'I--I was not expecting you. I came in here to--to think quietly
over something.'</p>
<p>He cannot quite keep out of his eyes the earnest wish that burns
behind them to know what that something is; but his hand is taken off
the chair, and his whole attitude a <hi rend="italic">going</hi>
one.</p>
<p>She glances up at him with what he feels to be an acutely painful,
strange shyness, while in her heart, through the new veil of shame and
shrinking, begins to rise the old longing for his sympathy.</p>
<p>'Something <hi rend="italic">has</hi> happened.'</p><pb n="314
DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Something that you had rather not tell me?'</p>
<p>'No-o. You would--everybody would have to know it soon. I have left
More Mansions.'</p>
<p>'For good?'</p>
<p>'Yes. Faustina and I have quarrelled!' </p>
<p>He forbids his face to express how little the arrival of this
d&#x00E9;nouement surprises him, and tries to look only
sympathetic.</p>
<p>'Irremediably?'</p>
<p>'Oh, yes--yes!'</p>
<p>She has sat down again at the table, and her distress--her need for
comfort--is so obvious that he cannot resist the temptation to sit
down, too.</p>
<p>'I am so sorry. Quarrels are such mistakes, are not they? Could I
be of any use? Could not you use me as a go-between?'</p>
<p>This suggestion, to  his consternation, drowns her in
crimson.</p><pb n="315     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">You!</hi> Oh, no--no!' Then, feeling how
inevitably the violent unwisdom of her disclaimer must have made him
draw the inference that he himself was the object of contention, she
rushes into a true, though misleading, admission. 'We quarrelled
about Cressida Delafield.'</p>
<p>'Indeed.'</p>
<p>'I told Faustina that she must choose between her and me.'</p>
<p>'And she chose Miss Delafield?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>He is silent, afraid to seem as if he would push into her
confidence--a reticence the less meritorious since he knows that,
having gone so far, she must go further, and unable to feign an
astonishment that he is far from feeling.</p>
<p>'I ought to tell you that my making this stipulation was not due to
a petty jealousy, as you might think, but to Faustina's having
persuaded the girl to leave her parents.'</p><pb n="316     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Faustina has not much opinion of parents'--dryly--'but why?'</p>
<p>'In order that she may devote her life to--'</p>
<p>'To <hi rend="italic">what?</hi>'</p>
<p>Althea hesitates, divided between her native maiden shrinking from
embarking on so scabrous a topic with a young man and the teaching of
the last months, which has instructed her that all topics are to be
handled indifferently between the sexes. It is not the latter, after
all, which produces her low answer:</p>
<p>'To rescue work.'</p>
<p>'To <hi rend="italic">what?</hi>'</p>
<p>'To rescue work'--still lower.</p>
<p>For a second he stares at her in stupefaction. Then:</p>
<p>'We cannot be referring to the same person. I thought you were
alluding to the young lady whom I met at Lady Lavinia Jerome's
party.'</p><pb n="317     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'And who complimented you upon your speech to the dockers--so I
am.'</p>
<p>'That <hi rend="italic">child!</hi>'</p>
<p>'She is not such a child as you think; she is twenty-one.'</p>
<p>He still looks bewildered.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Rescue work!</hi> Why, even Faustina--and do I understand that when you
remonstrated with her she refused to listen to you?'</p>
<p>'She insulted me grossly.'</p>
<p>Again that smarting blush smites her like a blow, and her voice
grows rigid again.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Insulted you grossly--how?</hi>'</p>
<p>'I cannot tell you--you must never ask me!'--almost inaudibly.</p>
<p>His face hardens, and he stands up.</p>
<p>'Then, I will ask her!'</p>
<p>'I will never speak to you again if you do!' She, too, has started
to her feet, but, recalled to herself by the publicity of the place,
and still more by the unbounded wonder in Drake's eyes, sits down
again.<pb n="318     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> 'I mean to say that it is no
question of <hi rend="italic">me</hi>; that after--after what has passed, it would be useless
to try and patch up a reconciliation between me and Faustina!'</p>
<p>An overpowering wind of recollection seems to bow her head, and she
bends before it. She looks such a monument of woe that even his
curiosity fades before his earnest desire to succour her.</p>
<p>'And is there nothing that you will let me do to help you?'</p>
<p>'I do not know what there is that <hi rend="italic">you</hi>--that anyone can do.'</p>
<p>Silenced for the moment by this finality of affliction, he can only
send mute messages of cautious sympathy across her unbroken wheat-cake
to her, and when he does speak, it is to make a homely suggestion.</p>
<p>'Had not you better eat something?'</p>
<p>'I could not; it would stick in my throat.'</p>
<p>'If you will forgive my asking you, have you made any plan--thought
out at all what<pb n="319     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> it will be best for you
to do for--just the present--for now?'</p>
<p>'No.'</p>
<p>'But you will have to make up your mind--to take some step, will
not you?'</p>
<p>'Oh, I suppose so.'</p>
<p>There is such cold dismissal of the topic in her tone that he dares
not pursue it.</p>
<p>Presently she begins to stir restlessly; to look about her for the
gloves whose absence she has forgotten; to show feverish signs of
departure.</p>
<p>'Are you going?'</p>
<p>'I am wasting time, and there is none to lose. I must take some
other step. I cannot leave that girl to her fate.'</p>
<p>There is a painful look of wool-gathering in her white face, which
shows her still half stunned from her recent blow. Destitute as he is
of any right to prevent her, he cannot allow her to set forth on an
enterprise for which she is so plainly unfit. He interposes<pb
n="320     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> himself between her and the door, towards
which she has turned.</p>
<p>'Will not you eat something first?'</p>
<p>'I tell you it would stick in my throat if I did.'</p>
<p>'Will not you at least sit down again for a moment, and let us talk
it over quietly?'</p>
<p>'What good would talking it over quietly do?'</p>
<p>'We might strike out something. You might see your way to let me
help you.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">You?</hi> Oh no!'</p>
<p>At any other time her emphasis of negation would have hurt him;
now, in the concentration of his eagerness to stop her, he passes it
by.</p>
<p>'You might at least let me use whatever influence I have with
Faustina.'</p>
<p>Her only answer is a--to him--incomprehensible shudder.</p>
<p>'Perhaps you doubt my possessing any; but I really have
some.'</p><pb n="321     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>She has collapsed into her chair again--not because convinced by
his arguments, but unable to trust her knocking knees. With an effort
she collects her swimming thoughts to answer him.</p>
<p>'You did not seem to have much when you tried to persuade her about
the chromate of potash.'</p>
<p>'That is true.'</p>
<p>Her shaking fingers begin to fidget with the spoon of her
coffee-cup.</p>
<p>'I must think of something else. I must do something--do
something--at once.'</p>
<p>'I cannot see that you have any responsibility in the matter.'</p>
<p>'Oh yes, I have--there are reasons. And, besides, her mother--I met
her this morning; did I tell you that it was from her I heard the
news?--lays all the blame upon <hi rend="italic">me!</hi>'</p>
<p>'Upon <hi rend="italic">you?</hi>'</p>
<p>'Yes. She says that if it had not been for my fatal
example--'</p><pb n="322     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Her throat seems to close.</p>
<p>'I would treat such gross injustice with the contempt it
deserves'--indignantly.</p>
<p>'I am not so sure that it is unjust.'</p>
<p>Seeing her thus resolute to heap ashes on her own head, he resumes
the path of practical suggestion.</p>
<p>'Would it be any use for you to appeal to Miss Delafield
herself?'</p>
<p>'Not the slightest.'</p>
<p>'Or to your own people--your own family? I think they are
acquainted with her?'</p>
<p>'No, <hi rend="italic">no!</hi> They are the last people who must hear a word of it!'</p>
<p>Such a frenzy of opposition shrills in her answer to this last
proposal that he looks round nervously; but the denizens of this
A. B. C., like those of all others, are <hi rend="italic">stoking</hi> themselves stolidly,
unmindful of their neighbours' concerns.</p>
<p>'And you think the matter urgent--need<pb n="323     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>ing instant action? You think that Faustina will--'</p>
<p>She snatches the sentence away from him, as if unable to bear any
ending he can put to it.</p>
<p>'Yes, I know it! She never lets the grass grow under her feet.'</p>
<p>A hopeless pause. Trivial but tenderly compassionate speculations
cross Drake's mind as to what she has done with her gloves, coupled
with the sudden perception that she looks ten years older than she
did when he parted from her last night.</p>
<p>'I should be the last person to press my suggestion, if you had a
better one to offer,' he says at last, with deprecating respect; 'but
if you have not, I think I should advise you to let me try my luck
with Faustina.'</p>
<p>She looks at him desperately. Her mind seems a boiling cauldron,
full of whirling thoughts, which she tries in vain to arrest and sort.
After awhile a kind of order<pb n="324     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> comes into
the chaos, and from it issues a voice which tells her that in this
proposal--the most repugnant that could possibly have been made to
her--lies her only chance of averting the threatened evil. Dares she
reject it? Through a species of woolly fog, her companion's voice,
still urging, reaches her.</p>
<p>'I really have some influence with her, though I do not wonder at
your doubting it; but if I put pressure on, I really have a good
deal.'</p>
<p>Silence. Her thoughts are clearing, and out of them rises in odious
distinctness a horrid picture of Drake confronting Faustina--of her
own name bandied about between them, sullied by the calumnies with
which Miss Bateson had so freely bespattered herself, and of which she
will certainly not be more sparing to her advocate--that advocate
whose very partisanship will give a plausible colour to her
accusations. And yet what<pb n="325     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> alternative
from this agony of degradation lies open to her? In the extremity of
her misery she hides her face.</p>
<p>'I do not know what to do; I am at my wits' end.'</p>
<p>He stands beside her patiently waiting, marvelling at, and yet
trying not even in his own mind to probe, the reasons of her anguished
shrinking from his proposal. When he sees her a little calmer he
gently repeats it:</p>
<p>'I think you had better trust me to do what I can for you.'</p>
<p>For a second or two she yields to the infinite sense of relief of
having someone to lean on; then Faustina's venomed phrases, flashing
back, poison the infant fountain of her comfort at its source.</p>
<p>'I could not bear it--I could not bear it!'</p>
<p>At the obstinacy of her apparent unreason his patience gives way a
little.</p>
<p>'I am afraid I have nothing else to suggest.'</p><pb n="326
DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>She lifts her forlorn head quickly. Is he going? Dreadful as is his
presence beside her, she suddenly realizes how much more dreadful his
leaving her will be.</p>
<p>'If--I--consent to what you propose, will you--will you--promise not to--not to listen
--to--stop your ears to--any--any--any insulting accusations that she
may bring against me?'</p>
<p>It would be invidious to say which was the more highly coloured,
the young woman at making this suggestion, or the young man at hearing
it.</p>
<p>'Is not it an insult to me to exact such a promise?'</p>
<p>'Oh that it should have come to this!' she says, the memory of her
former infatuation wringing a little low cry out of her at its so
ignominious ending.</p>
<p>'I would not think about that, if I were you, now.'</p>
<p>She heaves a great sigh, and then draws<pb n="327     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> her scattered wits together, as if trying to take his
advice.</p>
<p>'If you--really mean--to carry out--your--your suggestion, I
suppose it had better be--at once. She is not a person who ever loses
time; and she may be meaning to put her--her scheme into execution
to-ni--at once.'</p>
<p>Deep repugnance and fevered hurry strive together in her speech,
and the pitiful conflict stirs him to an even tenderer compassion than
he has yet felt.</p>
<p>'You may depend upon me. Should I find her at home this
afternoon?'</p>
<p>'I think so; she was evidently expecting Cressida.' The corners of
her mouth go down, pulled at by a very bitter recollection, and he
looks at her with silent commiseration. 'She has an engagement for
this evening. As it is a long way off, she will set off early; so you
had better be on the safe side, and go now.'</p><pb n="328     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Drake's heart gives a throb of pleasure at her taking his eagerness
to serve her so much as a matter of course as to need neither apology
nor thanks; but there is no sign of it in his answer.</p>
<p>'I will go as soon as I know what is to become of you.'</p>
<p>'Of me?'</p>
<p>'Yes, of you.'</p>
<p>'Oh, I do not know!'</p>
<p>'You must decide upon something; the day is getting on.'</p>
<p>'Yes; I suppose so.'</p>
<p>'Are your people in town?'</p>
<p>'As far as I know.'</p>
<p>'Your sister, Mrs. Boteler?'</p>
<p>'I dare say.'</p>
<p>'Will not it be best for you to be with your own family?'</p>
<p>'My family disapproves of me a good deal. It seems to me that most
people disapprove of me.'</p><pb n="329     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Do they disapprove of you enough to turn you away from their
door?'</p>
<p>Her answer is a tarrying one. Do they? He had put the question as
propounding an absurdity; but to her it seems quite within the range
of possibility that they should. For weeks she has kept away from
Clare, deterred by that long-unfulfilled promise; and now that what
her family will look upon as the result of her bad faith has broken
in thunder upon them, how can she venture to present herself before
them? A dreadful vision of Edward confronting her in loud or, still
more terrible, speechless wrath under Clare's palms rises before her
swimming eyes.</p>
<p>'I do not know; they may. Oh, how I wish'--catching at a
straw--'that I could go to Canning Town!'</p>
<p>'I am afraid that is not possible to-day.'</p>
<p>'Could not that nice couple take me in?'</p>
<p>'I am afraid not.'</p><pb n="330     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>His words are chilling, but the throb at his heart is louder than
before. She heaves another prodigious sigh, and once again looks about
mechanically for her absent gloves.</p>
<p>'Then, I suppose there is no help for it.'</p>
<p>'Shall I call you a hansom?'</p>
<p>'If you like.'</p>
<p>He is so afraid of her vacillating away once again from the only
sensible plan which it is in her power to adopt, that he gives her no
time to change her mind, and in another minute a cab stands at the
door. She submits passively while he puts her in, forgetting even that
she has not paid for her coffee; and at first it seems as if she were
to be packed off like a parcel, and without any more power of
utterance than if she had been wrapped in brown paper and tied with
string. But the noise of the flaps, which he stands upon the step to
shut down upon her, seems to give her back her speech.</p><pb
n="331     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You will let me hear, whatever there is to hear, <hi rend="italic">at once</hi>.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">At once</hi>.'</p>
<p>'And you will not believe--you will try not to believe--'</p>
<p>The wheels drown what he is not to believe, but he knows it pretty
well.</p><milestone unit="section" rend="stars"/>
<p>Althea drives along through a mist, although the sun is showing to
foreigners all and sundry what he can do, when he is put to it, in the
way of shining upon that town whose chimneys are believed to have
bested him. It is the fullest time of the afternoon, and a block often
brings her to a standstill. She sees that people are looking harder
at her than usual, and, though accustomed to being stared at for her
prettiness, feels that there is something different to-day.</p>
<p>Clare's butler--he is new since her last visit--announces to her
with apparent pleasure that his mistress is 'Not at home,'<pb
n="332     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> and, when she feebly says that she will
come in and wait, looks respectfully doubtful.</p>
<p>'Mrs. Boteler is not very well, 'm, and her horders were that no
one, with the exception of one or two hintimate friends, was to be
admitted.'</p>
<p>'I am one of the exceptions. I am Mrs. Boteler's sister.'</p>
<p>At that he ceases his opposition, and she follows him, quakingly
asking herself whether she has indeed spoken truth.</p>
<p>Clare is lying on the sofa, and Althea has time for one moment of
poignant anxiety as to what emotion she shall see succeeding the first
inevitable one of surprise before Mrs. Boteler jumps up, with no
appearance of ill-health, and runs to meet her.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">At last!</hi>' she cries. 'Now, are not you ashamed of yourself?'</p>
<p>The reproach is so gay and gentle, and applies so obviously to no
worse crime than her having absented herself, that Althea,<pb
n="333     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> breaking down under the reaction, bursts
into tears.</p>
<p>'My dear, how ill you look! What is it?'</p>
<p>The other's sobs make her scarcely intelligible.</p>
<p>'I have come--to ask--whether--you will take me--take me--in.'</p>
<p>The arms instantly clasped round her thin shoulders would be answer
enough, even without the galloping response:</p>
<p>'There is so much need to ask that, is not there?'</p>
<p>She draws the humbled girl down on the sofa beside her, and, not
teasing her with questions, waits for her to explain herself. But
Althea's first words have no relation to herself.</p>
<p>'You were lying down. Are not you well?'</p>
<p>Clare blushes slightly.</p>
<p>'I never felt better in my life; but, you know, William is so fussy
about me.'</p><pb n="334     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Althea stares stupidly at her. The squalid tornado that has rent
her life seems to have blown away half her wits.</p>
<p>'And--and the others? How is--how is Fanny?'</p>
<p>'Fanny is a tremendous success.'</p>
<p>The figures of Pharaoh's butler and baker, with their unexplained
variety of fate, rise quaintly before Althea's dimmed mental eye, the
one with his head lifted up, and the other hanged. Fanny a tremendous
success, and <hi rend="italic">she!</hi></p>
<p>'I must say that, if I am tired, William is
<hi rend="italic">excellent</hi> about taking her to balls.'</p>
<p>A trivial vision of William with his arm chronically twined round
Fanny's waist displaces the butler and baker before the eyes of
William's sister-in-law, lessening the virtue of his sacrifice, but
does not detain her a moment from the real subject of her
preoccupation.</p>
<p>'And--and Ned?'</p><pb n="335     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Mrs. Boteler's soft face stiffens a little.</p>
<p>'He has not been up for some weeks; he is reading hard for
Greats. Poor fellow! he realizes that work is the best thing for
him.'</p>
<p>Tone and words are dry, and <hi rend="italic">tant soit peu</hi>
reproachful, but to Althea they bring an untold relief. He knows
nothing; he has heard nothing.  That terrible vision of a brother
vengefully confronting her is only a figment of her own brain. For the
moment, at least, she may let herself go to the unspeakable ease and
solace of this reached haven. Her tired head falls back on the
sofa-cushion, and the water stands again in her eyes. Her whole look
is so bruised and pitiful that the other's conscience smites her for
her transient severity.</p>
<p>'I see that something very bad has happened. Do not you think you
could tell me what it is?'</p>
<p>At the delicate kindness of this inquiry the shower falls. </p><pb
n="336     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You have left her?'</p>
<p>A speechless nod.</p>
<p>'For good?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'You have quarrelled with her?'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'And you never mean to go back?'</p>
<p>'God forbid!'</p>
<p>A crescendo of cautious but eager cheerfulness has marked
Mrs. Boteler's questions, and at the energy of this last disclaimer
she flings both arms again round her sister's neck.</p>
<p>'Oh, I <hi rend="italic">am</hi> glad! Do not be angry with me, but
I <hi rend="italic">am</hi> glad! I knew that you must find out in
time what a fraud she is; but I feared it might be a long while first.'</p>
<p>'Do not call her names!' cries Althea, with a shiver of stung
loyalty to her broken ideal. 'I loved her dearly; I believed in
her--oh, <hi rend="italic">how</hi> I believed in her!--but I have
been dreadfully--<hi rend="italic">dreadfully</hi>
disillusioned.'</p><pb n="337     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Since when?'</p>
<p>Althea heaves a sigh of deep humiliation.</p>
<p>'I can see now that it has been coming for a long time, that she
has been growing sick of me; but it culminated this morning when I
remonstrated with her about something she was going to do, which I
thought absolutely criminal.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Criminal!</hi>'</p>
<p>Clare's eyes sparkle at the thought of Miss Bateson having placed
herself within the clutch of the law.</p>
<p>'Morally criminal, I mean.'</p>
<p>'And her answer was, to turn you out of doors?'</p>
<p>'I turned myself out. I could not stay to hear any more such--such
outrages as she was heaping upon me.'</p>
<p>Clare reddens in sympathy with the scarlet that has bathed her
sister.</p>
<p>'I always felt that there were great possibilities of Billingsgate
latent in her.'</p><pb n="338     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I left all my things behind me; I did not even'--with a
half-scared look at her hands--'remember to take my gloves.'</p>
<p>'I will send for them at once'--rising and ringing the bell. 'If I
do not'--with a burst of disgust and anger--'she will probably pawn
them.'</p>
<p>And again Althea shivers.</p></div>

<pb n="[339]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="16"><head>CHAPTER XVI.</head>
<p>THE servant sent to recover Miss Vane's wardrobe from the
apprehended pawnshop returns in time for her to appear in her own
clothes at her sister's dinner-table. It is not likely that at the
height of the season she will find her relations dining alone; but
she has been too self-absorbed to realize this, and, on finding that
she will have to face strangers, begs off appearing. But Clare gently
discourages the proposal.</p>
<p>'They are only men; and so William will take you in. I will tell
him not to talk much to you.'</p>
<p>'I am afraid I shall be rather a kill-joy.'</p><pb n="340     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> 
<p>'Oh no, you will not. There are only three or four old Etonians
come up for the match.'</p>
<p>'What match?'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">What match</hi>'--laughingly mimicking her. 'You
had better not let William and Fanny hear you. Do you mean to say you
do not know that it is the first day of the Eton and Harrow?'</p>
<p>Nothing can be kinder than William's greeting when they meet in the
drawing-room before the arrival of the guests.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Very</hi> glad to see you!' he says, shaking her
hand almost as heartily as if it had been Fanny's.</p>
<p>There is an intention to kiss her in his eye, but something in her
manner makes him abandon it, and substitute the not particularly
felicitous remark:</p>
<p>'I thought we should end by rescuing you from the shrieking
sisterhood.'</p>
<p>His wife, standing near, puts in a gently<pb n="341     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> hasty 'We will not talk about that,' which diverts her
husband's attention to herself, making him ply her with what seem to
Althea very teasing questions, as to whether she has obeyed his
injunctions in lying long enough on the sofa; whether she is sure she
has not seen too many people, etc. Fanny next claims his attention,
her toilet demanding a good deal of facetious criticism and some
fingering, so that, on the whole, the returned truant tells herself
that, considering what William is, she has come off pretty
cheaply.</p>
<p>And there is real kindness in his 'Now that we have got you, we
shall not let you go in a hurry,' as he presses the fingers that rest
on his arm against his side during their downward march to the
dining-room.</p>
<p>He relapses into funniness two or three times during dinner--as
when, with a glance at her collar-bones, he expresses a playful wonder
that two such radicals as she and her<pb n="342     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
friend should have dined so often with Duke Humphrey. But for the most
part, in obedience, probably, to his wife's orders, he leaves her in
peace.</p>
<p>The conversation rolls almost wholly upon the match, and
Mr. Boteler throws his bad jokes upon it about the table--jokes which
Fanny receives with low bursts of ecstatic laughter, such as, indeed,
she bestows upon the sallies of all the other men. Fanny has no
repartee, and does much better without a gift which in general brings
to its possessor, if a woman, neither love nor money.</p>
<p>The absolute aloofness of the interests about her from that one
which has been tyrannizing her whole being makes Althea feel
inexpressibly stupid. It is with difficulty that she can keep enough
wits about her to produce the 'Yes' or 'No' occasionally asked of her
in their right places; to abstract herself for even a moment from the
devouring fever of her apprehensions as to how her<pb n="343     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> messenger is prospering on that mission, upon which seems
to her to hang whatever of peace may be in store for her future
life. How soon is it possible for her to hear the result of Drake's
quest?</p>
<p>As time wears on, her preoccupation becomes more and more
painful. The ladies have returned to the drawing-room, and Fanny, with
a thoughtful husbanding of the charms which are to be exhibited at two
balls, curls herself up on a sofa and goes to sleep, after prettily
saying how too pleasant for words it is to have Althea's company
again. The other sister, with a nicer observation and a sincerer
solicitude, urges the jaded girl to go to bed.</p>
<p>'No, no; I cannot. I should not sleep! I will stay, at all events,
until Fanny goes to dress.'</p>
<p>There is such a strange excitement in her manner that Clare looks
at her alarmed and puzzled.</p><pb n="344     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You are not--not expecting--anyone?'</p>
<p>'No.'</p>
<p>Presently the men come up, and Fanny wakes just in time to shake
out her ruffled plumes and stroll on to the balcony with one or two of
them, pleasing their ears with her little observations on the stars,
which make them feel quite clever.</p>
<p>William devotes himself to his other sister-in-law, and plays with
somewhat clumsy variations upon the kindly theme of his determination
not to let her go again now he has got her, and his congratulations
and rejoicings over her recovered reason. She scarcely hears him, the
heightening distress of her mind making her deaf to any other
theme.</p>
<p>It is growing evident that she will not learn her fate to-night;
that she will have to bridge the enormous chasm that parts her from
another day with sleepless hours of unrelieved suspense. The
telegraph-offices must<pb n="345     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> long have been
closed, for is not midnight nearing?</p>
<p>Fanny has pecked her good-night upon her sisters' cheeks, and
danced away to the brougham; and William, lingering to impress fondly
fussy orders upon his wife not to stay up chattering, has
followed.</p>
<p>'We do not feel at all inclined to disobey him; do we?' says Clare,
with a pitying glance at her sister's white face.</p>
<p>But the other utterly repudiates the hope of slumber.</p>
<p>'I should like to sit up all night.'</p>
<p>'Do not you sleep?'</p>
<p>'If I do not, that is no reason for keeping you out of bed.'</p>
<p>She follows Clare upstairs with dragging limbs.</p>
<p>'I will not come in, though it is a sore temptation,' says
Mrs. Boteler, pausing at the threshold of her sister's door; 'but I
should never hear the last of it if I did'--<pb n="346     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>smiling. 'Sleep well, and do not come down to breakfast.'
</p>
<p>She turns reluctantly, as if loath to leave anything so
uncomforted; and the next moment Althea hears her voice speaking to
the butler, who has apparently followed her upstairs--'For me?' and
his answer: 'No; for Miss Vane.'</p>
<p>In an instant Althea has sprung into the passage, and snatched the
telegram out of the man's hand, not heeding his explanation: 'It was
left by mistake at No. 24, and has only just been sent in.'</p>
<p>Though in such haste to open it, a moment or two passes before she
can master its import, though the message is of the
briefest.</p><milestone unit="section" rend="stars"/>
<p>It is through no dilatoriness on the part of Drake that Miss Vane
has been kept so long upon her gridiron. No sooner has he put her into
one hansom than he puts himself into a second, and gives the
familiar<pb n="347     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> address, '4, More Mansions.'
Not only with the object of arresting Faustina at the earliest period,
but because he knows that the more he looks at his errand the less he
will like it, does he thus bustle its fulfilment.</p>
<p>Drake has no particular objection--adequate cause given--to a row
with one of his own sex; but, like all other able-bodied,
healthy-minded men, anything in the nature of a quarrel with a woman
is extremely distasteful to him.</p>
<p>Faustina herself opens the door, as she had done earlier in the day
to Althea.</p>
<p>'I am "not at home,"' she says cavalierly, 'as I must go out in a
quarter of an hour; but you may come in for a minute or two.'</p>
<p>She leads the way to the drawing-room, which seems to his fancy
still to show marks of the morning's battle, as if that battle had
been one where literal instead of metaphorical missiles had
hurtled.</p>
<p>Miss Bateson has no more opinion of<pb n="348     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
order and neatness in her surroundings than she has of filial piety,
reverence, etc., and to the young man's eyes the absence of Althea's
refining and straightening influence is already perceptible.</p>
<p>'You are apt to come at inconvenient moments; but I am not sorry to
see you,' Faustina says, and so holds out her hand.</p>
<p>His makes no answering motion.</p>
<p>'What does this mean?'</p>
<p>He had been doubtful whether the bluff offhandness of her manner
had not concealed some suspicion of his purpose; but her air of
apparently unaffected surprise staggers him.</p>
<p>'It means that shaking hands implies a friendly relation, and that
it is with no friendly feeling that I come to you to-day.'</p>
<p>The surprise, whether real or only well counterfeited, passes out
of her eyes, and she sits down.</p>
<p>'If we are going to say unpleasant things<pb n="349     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> to each other, we may as well do it comfortably.'</p>
<p>'Thank you, I had rather stand.'</p>
<p>'As you please.'</p>
<p>There is a slight pause, both combatants arming. It is, perhaps, a
false move on the part of Faustina that it is she who gives the signal
to fire. It is, at all events, a relief to her antagonist.</p>
<p>'I gather that Althea has been visiting you with her finger in her
eye.'</p>
<p>She laughs slightingly.</p>
<p>'Then, you gather what is absolutely false.'</p>
<p>Faustina shrugs her shoulders.</p>
<p>'She has been communicating with you--the method is
unimportant. You cannot deny that, I suppose?'</p>
<p>'I see no reason for introducing her name into the discussion.'</p>
<p>'If they have no reference to her, I am quite at a loss to guess
the meaning of these heroics.' Her voice is contemptuous, and she<pb
n="350     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> half strangles a yawn. 'And time is
short,' she adds, with a meaning glance clockwards.</p>
<p>'It will be long enough for me,' he says, stung by her tone; 'I
shall not detain you long. I have only one brief request--one demand
to make of you.'</p>
<p>'And that is?'</p>
<p>'That you will abandon the at once nefarious and ridiculous scheme
with regard to Miss Delafield that I hear you have framed.'</p>
<p>The answer takes a moment before it can come as smilingly as its
utterer wishes.</p>
<p>'You have said your lesson well, and you have almost as much
command of language as your--your <hi rend="italic">employer</hi>;
but, as you know, I have never objected to plain speaking, and I
should be glad if you would tell me what inducement you hold out to me
to comply with a request which may seem to me as ridiculous and
nefarious as my project does to you.'</p><pb n="351     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'What inducement!' he repeats slowly, as if the shape of the
question made it difficult to him for the moment to answer it.</p>
<p>'Or perhaps I should rather say, what deterrent to frighten me from
it.'</p>
<p>He pauses for a second.</p>
<p>'The absolute and glaring unfitness of the tool for the task--has
that no weight with you?'</p>
<p>'I deny your premise. If I had not thought the tool fitted for the
task, I should not have picked it out.'</p>
<p>'The misery entailed upon the girl's family?'</p>
<p>She shakes her head.</p>
<p>'You know what my opinions are as to the so-called rights of
parents to mutilate and cramp their children's lives. You may forget
the fact; but you once shared them.'</p>
<p>He passes by the personal application with quiet contempt.</p>
<p>'The horrors to which you expose her?'</p><pb n="352     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>She smiles.</p>
<p>'You may keep your breath to cool your porridge, and your rhetoric
for a paragraph in a society paper. Have you yet to learn that with me
the implement is always a most secondary consideration, and is
esteemed solely as it may lend its polish or its blade to the service
of the Cause?'</p>
<p>He puts out his hand impatiently.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Connu!</hi> I have heard it before. Save it for
someone to whom it is fresher.'</p>
<p>Her good-humour, or at least her self-command, seems proof even
against this shaft.</p>
<p>'Have you come to the bottom of your bag of bombs?' she asks
jeeringly.</p>
<p>'Not quite; I have one or two left.'</p>
<p>Something in the look of his face or the determination of his
manner makes her vaguely restless.</p>
<p>She takes up a paper-knife and balances it on her fingers. It was
an early love-token<pb n="353     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> from Althea, and
has <hi rend="italic">Auf Ewig</hi> foolishly slanting across its
blade in gilt letters; but neither of them notices this. He looks
down at her calmly before again speaking; and she, suddenly feeling
that the inequality of their levels is giving him an advantage over
her, rises and, standing firmly on her well-planted feet, draws up her
tall stature.</p>
<p>'You are very self-confident,' he says, with an inflection that
sounds almost one of pity--'very sure of yourself. It is a valuable
quality, but it may land you in a morass.'</p> 
<p>'Would you mind keeping to the text, or shall we have the rest of
the sermon another day?'</p>
<p>Her voice is still a jeering one, but there has come into it an
indefinable accent of alarm.</p>
<p>'Have you reflected what a hornets' nest you will bring about your
ears by provoking the enmity of a family as powerful by connection and
social standing as Miss Delafield's?'</p><pb n="354     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'What harm can they do to me? The claws have been pared and the
fangs drawn of such as they this many a year.'</p>
<p>Again he halts for a moment. She is so close to him that he can
feel her breath on his cheek, and knows that it is coming hot and
anxiously.</p>
<p>'They could make the place too hot to hold you.' He waits a moment
for this statement to have time to sink well in, and then adds: 'I
think you would find other people beside me withdrawing from your
acquaintance.'</p>
<p>'You are threatening me with the loss of your acquaintance?'</p>
<p>'I am threatening nothing. I am simply telling you what will be the
result of your action.'</p>
<p>'It comes to the same thing. You are implying that you will
withdraw your acquaintance--what I used once humorously to call your
friendship--from me if I persist.'</p><pb n="355     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'What you used humorously to call my friendship for you--yes.'</p>
<p>Her next question comes heralded and, as it were, delayed by a dark
blush:</p>
<p>'That means, in plain English, that you will withdraw the help--the
pecuniary help--which you have given me all these years; given by you
and accepted by me without humiliation, because we were both in the
same boat.'</p>
<p>'We were never in the same boat.'</p>
<p>'We were in the same boat, inasmuch as we had both been turned out
of doors for our fidelity to our opinions.'</p>
<p>'Was it for <hi rend="italic">your opinions</hi> that you were
turned out of doors?'</p>
<p>He looks at her piercingly, well in the eyes, and hers, after
trying to brazen it out for an instant, drop.</p>
<p>'It was for carrying, or trying to carry, them to their logical
conclusion,' she answers; but though there is defiance, there is
also<pb n="356     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> fear in her tone. The young man
shrugs his shoulders contemptuously.</p>
<p>'I have no wish to stir up that old mud. I helped you because I
could not see an old playmate starve; because I believed that
injustice had been meted out to you--that your convictions <hi rend="italic">were</hi>
convictions, although they had led you into extravagant and immoral
action--'</p>
<p>She breaks in, unable--though conscious of the ticklish nature of
her situation--to deny herself the poignant pleasure of a gibe:</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Extravagant and immoral!</hi> Give me time to enjoy this new
strain. Since when has this admiring loyalty to the Marriage Laws
blossomed out in you?'</p>
<p>He does what it is always wise, and almost as always difficult, to
do in the case of angry speech, passes it by, continuing his own theme
as if she had not spoken.</p>
<p>'All these years I have been trying to<pb n="357     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> keep my belief in you--a belief that, under all the puff
and push and vulgar striving for notoriety, there still existed
something of the real thing--some grain of selfless love, of righteous
anger, of noble faith; but during the last months that belief has been
daily growing weaker, and to-day it has died.'</p>
<p>His voice has throughout been neither loud nor vituperative,
despite the stinging severity of his words, and through the last
clause of his speech there runs an intonation of sadness. Her answer
begins in bluster:</p>
<p>'What is it to me whether you or such as you weary me with your
stupid belief, or insult me with your stupider disbelief?' Then, as he
continues to hold her with the quiet determination of his eye, she
changes her tone: 'It would be more to the purpose if, instead of
slanging me, you were to treat me to a practical statement of what it
is that you wish me to do.'</p><pb n="358     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I wish you to sit down at once and write a note to Miss
Delafield.'</p>
<p>'Dictated by you?'</p>
<p>'If you prefer it.'</p>
<p>He has baffled her by taking her derisive question as if seriously
asked, and for a moment she hesitates.</p>
<p>'And supposing that I refuse?'</p>
<p>'I think you will repent it.'</p>
<p>'Supposing that I fling your petty help in your face, and defy
you?'</p>
<p>He wisely leaves this query to answer itself, which after a while
it does, by its author walking slowly to the writing-table and sitting
down at it. Her self-respect is almost as much restored by the
utterance of her threat of renunciation as if she had carried it out,
and it is with what she feels to be real dignity that, when seated,
she turns to him.</p>
<p>'You have interfered in a matter with which you have no smallest
concern; you<pb n="359     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> have stooped to be the
tool of a girl as contemptible in character as puny in intellect; you
have used a lever which no generous mind would have employed; and now,
will you please tell me what I am to say?'</p></div>

<pb n="[360]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="17"><head>CHAPTER XVII.</head>
<p>'ALL right!'</p>
<p>When Althea's eyes allow her to read it, she finds that these two
words compose her wire.</p>
<p>She is standing lost in the immensity of her relief, when Clare's
voice sounds in her ear.</p>
<p>'No bad news, I hope?'</p>
<p>'Oh no--none.'</p>
<p>Mrs. Boteler has, after all, crossed her sister's threshold,
