prepared to throw William's prohibition to the winds on the smallest
encouragement. But she gets none, and after a moment or two retires,
rather reluctantly, but without putting any further question.</p><pb
n="361     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>Althea is left to the enjoyment of her rebound from suspense--an
enjoyment that at first seems perfect, but afterwards is nibbled at by
carping questions.</p>
<p>'All right!' What does it mean? How much ground does it cover? Is
the reprieve only a temporary one, or is the overhanging evil for ever
averted? If so, what means has Drake employed? By what lever has he
been able to remove the mountain of Faustina's purpose?</p>
<p>Over these problems she tosses most of the night--a regrettable
waste of time and tissue, since morning brings the solution of, at all
events, one of them in a letter from Drake himself:</p>
<q><text><body><div type="letter" id="rbdearf"><opener><salute>'DEAR
MISS VANE,</salute></opener>
<p>'I hope that the telegram I have just sent you will relieve your
anxiety. I am very pleased to say that I have been able to persuade
Miss Bateson permanently to<pb n="362     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> abandon her
project. She is leaving London at once for some little time, so that
you need not fear the disagreeableness of a meeting.</p>
<p>'Trusting that this will set your mind quite at rest,</p>
<p>'I am,</p><closer><signed>'Yours very truly,<name>'JOHN TRECOTHICK
DRAKE.'</name></signed></closer></div></body></text></q>
<p>She turns the page, to see whether there is nothing more on the
other side; but the postscript is not as sure a find in a man's letter
as in a woman's, and from this one it is altogether absent.</p>
<p>She reads the note again with deep breaths of relief as she goes
along. '<hi rend="italic">Permanently to abandon her project!</hi>'
How <hi rend="italic">has</hi> he done it? Oh, <hi rend="italic">what</hi> a relief! She can
face Ned again! Ned need never know! But how <hi
rend="italic">has</hi> he done it? He might have gone a little more
into detail.</p><pb n="363     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>She reads it a third time. Nothing--absolutely nothing but the bare
facts!</p>
<p>They, at least, are entirely satisfactory, thank God! but he must
have known how she would hunger for an explanation. He need not have
been quite so short, nor--with a fourth survey--quite so dry.</p>
<p>At that she takes herself up for carping at one who has just done
her such an unspeakable service. Ah! but in letting him do it has she
lost him? Has Faustina repeated to him the calumnies that had driven
her (Althea) blind and staggering into the public street? And has he,
in part at least, and against his will, believed them?</p>
<p>The question buries her face downwards in her pillows, so deep that
the light knock with which someone prefaces her entrance is unheard by
her. She jumps back to the consciousness that Clare, in a pretty
dressing-gown, and with a still prettier morning smile, is standing by
her bed.</p><pb n="364     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'Were you asleep? and do you always lie on your face?'</p>
<p>'Never.'</p>
<p>'I came to ask how you are.'</p>
<p>'How kind of you!'</p>
<p>'Did you sleep well?'</p>
<p>'Middling.'</p>
<p>'And had a satisfactory post?'</p>
<p>'A very small one.'</p>
<p>With a careless air, Althea's hand goes out towards the letter
lying face uppermost on the counterpane, and covers it.</p>
<p>'But a pleasant one?'</p>
<p>'Oh yes, quite pleasant.'</p>
<p>The elder sister makes a slight pause, as it expecting something
further; but nothing comes, and with a faint and very passing cloud on
her brightness she goes away.</p>
<p>All through that day Althea has the disagreeable consciousness that
Clare is naturally expecting some further explanation of the cause
that has thrown her upon William<pb n="365     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
Boteler's hospitality, expecting her to give a slight sketch of the
levin bolt that has split a friendship proudly warranted to outlive
the everlasting hills.</p>
<p>But such explanation, such sketch, Althea is absolutely incapable
of giving. Her deity lies in shivers, proved to have been no more a
deity than 'the brutish gods of Nile,' its godhood having never
existed save in the dulness of her own belief; but the days are yet
too recent, when from its shrine it sent out inspiration, and she
knelt in adoration on its altar-steps, for her to be able to face the
storm of well-merited stones that would assail her fallen Dagon were
she to explain to what a depth it had sunk.</p>
<p>She shivers away from the topic as often as she sees any approach
made to it; and Clare, after one or two delicate essays at a fuller
confidence, desists, hiding whatever disappointment she may feel under
the mantle of tender compassion in which she wraps the<pb n="366
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> strayed lamb. And, after all, she does not feel
much.</p>
<p>Althea has recovered her wits--that is all that really
matters--though by so mortifying a method that she naturally has no
great desire to talk of it; through the agency of plenty of new milk
and strong consomme she will soon also regain her looks and spirits;
and meanwhile it is kindest to let her alone.</p>
<p>Althea accepts with dumb gratitude this discreet and merciful mode
of treatment. For the first day or two she is still so numbed and
bruised, that she has little feeling save for the physical repose and
well-being that are to repair the ravages made even more by Faustina's
cruelty than by her cuisine.</p>
<p>The immensity of the relief from her apprehension is followed by a
proportionate reaction. She has explained to herself the brevity of
Drake's note by the natural hypothesis that he will call in person to
eke out the scantiness of his communication. Her brain<pb n="367
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> busies itself in a woolly way with the problem of how
to man&#x0153;uvre for him an opportunity to see her alone when he
does call. But no need for such man&#x0153;uvring arises, and the
first thing that lifts the girl out of her lethargy is the realization
that the days are going by, and that Drake has made no sign.</p>
<p>'She is a pretty girl, and you know I would do anything for either
of your sisters,' William says one day to his wife, with the natural
resentment of a <hi rend="italic">mauvais plaisant</hi> whose wit has miscarried; 'but I must
say that she is a bit of a wet-blanket.'</p>
<p>'I am afraid she is barely up to joking yet,' replies his wife
soothingly.</p>
<p>'She must be precious thin-skinned if she cannot stand a little
chaff. I thought she liked it.'</p>
<p>'So she will, I am sure, when she is herself again,' rejoins
Mrs. Boteler sweetly and sincerely.</p>
<p>But William is not to be so easily mollified,<pb n="368     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> and he goes off grumbling,' So unlike Fanny!'</p>
<p>It is with Fanny that he seeks comfort.</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">You</hi> will not burst into tears if I say anything a little amusing
to you, as Althea does?'</p>
<p>'Burst into tears!' echoes Fanny, with renovating surprise--'what
do you mean? You know that you always make me die of laughing. I do
not know how you manage it, but you do.'</p>
<p>His brow clears. But it is destined to be overcast again for the
same cause many times during the ensuing weeks, since it is as
impossible for William not to make jokes as it is for Althea to laugh
at them. In vain she tells herself that the part of the day during
which she is exposed to the fire of his pleasantries is so small that
her gratitude might pay to his hospitality the tribute of a little
mirth. The quality of his humour seems to have the faculty of
inevitably stiffening her muscles.</p><pb n="369     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>And if there is anything else about him that tries her more than
his fun, it is his tiresome solicitude about his wife's health--the
pushing of needless stools, and insisting on undesired sofas,
proclaiming as they do to each chance comer Clare's hopes of
maternity. The first two or three times that this occurs, Althea
glances at her sister with sympathetic indignation; but seeing her
with cheerful gratitude accept the superfluous footstool, and lie down
upon the sofa on which she had rather have sat upright, she withdraws
her unneeded compassion, and centres it all upon herself.</p>
<p>And in truth she is very unhappy. The recovery of her nerves from
the shock of the explosion is so incomplete as to leave an
irritability behind it which renders her difficult to live with.</p>
<p>The violent death by which her passionate love and reverence for
Faustina has perished has left a void which, as she gloomily tells<pb
n="370     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> herself, nothing can ever fill; her plan
of noble life is in ignoble shivers; and till intercourse with him has
ceased, she has not realized how much she had grown to lean upon
Drake. He had done his best for her, as his high heart always prompts
him to do for any suffering creature; but now that his task is ended
he has passed on from her to some other pain that needs him more.</p>
<p>Is there any such? She shakes her head. And these happy people into
whose lives she has thrust herself, only to take the edge off their
pleasantness, do not need her. Often it fills her with a grieved
surprise, that yet does not alter the case, to find how absolutely out
of touch she has grown with their interests.</p>
<p>Those months of face-to-faceness with the grimnesses of life appear
to have robbed her of all zest for its graces. And yet her whole
scheme of existence seems now to have been<pb n="371     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> so entirely bound up with Faustina's as to have
necessarily perished with her.</p>
<p>There is one person who could have helped her to reconstruct it, to
weave afresh the strands of the broken web; but he has thought it best
to abstain from meddling any further in her concerns. He has
probably, like Faustina, recognized her incapacity to grapple with any
real difficulties, to carry out any worthy task. If he had not, would
he at such a turning-point of her history have left her?</p>
<p>And meanwhile she will have to make some plan for her future
life. The place of resident sister-in-law, once so affectionately
offered her, is no longer vacant. Fanny has more than justified by
brilliant success her appointment to it, and a man would have to be
'either a wild beast or a god' who could desire the permanent presence
of <hi rend="italic">two</hi> 'in-laws' by his hearthstone.</p>
<p>Even if William came under one of these<pb n="372     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> heads, of which Althea sees no sign, the approaching; baby
will at its advent make an extra inmate impossible, or at least
highly inconvenient. She points this out to her sister, and Clare,
though sweetly and hospitably waving away the subject, does not deny
the fundamental truth of the proposition. Perhaps she is beginning to
realize that it is through Althea that have come to her the only
conjugal jars that have marred her bliss.</p>
<p>And Althea herself? With daily deepening gloom she realizes that
she has cast herself out of her own sphere, without having gained a
footing in any other. There is not a spot on earth where she is not a
superfluity.</p>
<p>The season draws towards it close, and London is nearing its most
smelly and gasping moment. William's anxieties about his wife are now
complicated by fears of the possible effect upon her of the unusual
heat, and his<pb n="373     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> fussy exertions to keep
Clare cool send up everybody else's temperature.</p>
<p>The villa at Wimbledon which is the outcome of his cares, and to
which the family now migrate from Saturday to Monday, is a sensible
relief to all. It is a large villa, with an Italian name, and charming
grounds that wander away into a pretty wood--a real wood, with
well-girthed trees and flourishing bracken. One might be a hundred
miles from London, which each visitor remarks as punctually as we all
annually comment with surprising surprise upon the lengthening days of
March and the drawing-in evenings of October. It is not too distant
from London for Fanny to accomplish her tale of balls from it, and on
Sundays limp Londoners are only too glad to avail themselves of its
green shades and its bamboo chairs.</p>
<p>It is a pleasant life, and for the first few days even Althea's
spirits feel the tonic of its cool charm; then, with returning
energies,<pb n="374     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> seems to come an added power
of tasting the bitterness of her own failure. Her irritation reaches
its culminating-point one Saturday afternoon, when Fanny, having been
slightly stung by a wasp in the morning--a calamity which has made
William spend himself in caresses and remedies--has been recompensed on
his return from the Stock Exchange by the present of a pair of
mechanical toys, bought with the object of distracting her attention
from her sufferings.</p>
<p>Fanny is fond of toys, and at once kneels delightedly down on the
veranda, and, winding up one, sets it off. William follows suit with
the other, and soon two pigs--a woolly white and a snuffy brown
one--are racing in short chopping gallop across the tiled floor to the
accompaniment of their owner's unbounded mirth. Althea laughs, too,
inevitably at the clicking, bumping, colliding swine, but at some
thought checks herself.</p>
<p>'Are not they too funny for words?' cries<pb n="375     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> Fanny, still kneeling, flushed and rapturous. 'Did you
ever see such archangels?'</p>
<p>'Come, do not be too strong-minded to smile once in a way,' adds
William waggishly.</p>
<p>If she complies, it is a little austerely.</p>
<p>'The fact is, I am always afraid to be amused at anything of the
sort until I know how they are made.'</p>
<p>Fanny sits back on her heels, opening her eyes.</p>
<p>'How they are made? What do you mean?'</p>
<p>'I mean that I like to know how much human suffering they imply.'
Then, seeing both her companions, with arrested gaiety, look to her
for explanation, she goes on: 'For instance, one would think that
children's balloons and indiarubber toys were harmless things, would
not one? Yet in the factories where they are made, where carbon
bisulphide is employed, the vapours are so noxious that workers have
been known to go<pb n="376     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> mad, and try to throw
themselves out of the windows.'</p>
<p>There is a rather dismal silence, and Althea proceeds further to
improve the occasion.</p>
<p>'It is as well to know how many tears go to make up one laugh, is
not it?'</p>
<p>'You are, at all events, resolved that there shall not be too much
laugh where <hi rend="italic">you</hi> are,' replies William rudely.</p>
<p>She retorts in the same tone, and for the first time their covert
exasperation with each other breaks out in too candid speech. Fanny
wisely slides away, and they are left to fight it out.</p>
<p>It is of no use that, in a paroxysm of subsequent remorse, Althea
flings herself at Clare's knees, crying:</p>
<p>'You had better let me go before I have quite spoilt all your
lives.'</p>
<p>'I am sure you do not mean to do it,' replies poor Mrs. Boteler
rather miserably.</p><pb n="377     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>The next day is Sunday, and by the afternoon, when the London
visitors begin to arrive, the brows of the family are
smoothed. William has injudiciously insisted upon formally
apologizing, which has made things much worse; but outwardly the
halcyon seems to brood.</p>
<p>Althea has ardently tried to stem the current of her
brother-in-law's too florid acknowledgments by the candid confession
of her own superior faultiness, and though the personal distaste for
him lasts, and must last, it is against herself that her whole
contrite soul is crying out during the morning service in the church
on the Common.</p>
<p>If hearts could be laid bare, what strangely various tributary
streams of confession would be seen flowing into that General one to
whose noble tune our lips weekly move! How much more of temper and
spite than real concern for the sufferers by the abuse she had so
superfluously dragged in had she<pb n="378     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> felt
yesterday! The fact so clumsily introduced is true, and can be matched
with hundreds of other heartrending ones of the same kind. But how
much more harm than good had she done by lugging it in so malapropos
by the head and shoulders! How full of alloy are her best motives! how
profitless her activities! how pitiable the outcome of both!</p>
<p>'I shall never do anything with my life,' she says to herself as
she walks home.</p>
<p>She repeats it in deeper dejection later in the afternoon as she
sits alone--since everyone else, both visitors and housemates, have
strayed away garden- and woodwards--at the deserted tea-table. It is
set in the verandah, where every variety of wicker chair and lounge
invites to repose. On the pleasant house the awnings are still lowered
against the heat, but a little lazy air comes from courting the gay
flower-beds to lift the hair of the drooping girl.</p><pb n="379
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> 
<p>She has been pouring out tea for Clare, having caught with
remorseful eagerness at even this poor little chance of being
useful. But now all, like Wordsworth's stag, have 'drunk their fill,'
and left her.</p>
<p>Her head, beautifully dressed by Clare's maid in the latest mode,
hangs over the back of her bamboo chair; her feet, in pale silk
stockings and broidered shoes, rest on the rung of a vacated seat near
her; and her faint-coloured gown, thin and expensive, drifts about her
as the light wind gently pulls at it. A more exquisite picture of
opulent idleness it vwould be difficult to see, or one more unlike
that working woman whom she had been so proud to call herself.</p>
<p>It is the sharp consciousness of this contrast, both to the setting
in which he had been wont to see her, and still more to the condition
in which he had last parted from her, that is for the first moment
uppermost in the jumble of feelings with which a late arrival<pb
n="380     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> from London is overwhelmed and silenced as
he now looks at her.</p>
<p>He has stepped, footman-led, through the wide-open drawing-room
windows, and, hearing steps, she lifts her head languidly, thinking
that it is the servants coming to take away the tea.</p></div>

<pb n="[381]"/><div id="rbdearf" type="chapter"
n="18"><head>CHAPTER XVIII.</head>
<p>THE discovery of her mistake brings her to her feet in a
second. Even in the hurry of springing up from a low chair, 'How
graceful!' is his thought. For awhile she stands, a silent
lily--silent as her sisters in the parterre--before him; then speaks
sighingly:</p>
<p>'You have been a long while in coming.'</p>
<p>They are far from being the words which she would have chosen, but
they seem to say themselves.</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'I began to think that you were not coming at all.'</p>
<p>'Did you?'</p><pb n="382     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I have been very anxious to see you'--a slight interval--'in order
to thank you.'</p>
<p>'You <hi rend="italic">did</hi> thank me.'</p>
<p>'Only on paper--for such a service!'</p>
<p>She stops, words running short, as they are apt to do when any
extra demand is made upon them.</p>
<p>'Indeed you are overrating it.'</p>
<p>'Overrating it! I wonder, have you any idea how great--how infinite
the relief was?'</p>
<p>'I hoped it would be.'</p>
<p>'I can never, <hi rend="italic">never</hi>, <hi
rend="smallcaps">never</hi> thank you enough! But how did you do it?
By what miracle? What arguments did you use?'</p>
<p>Her questions tumble over each other in her haste, but there is in
her companion no corresponding hurry to answer.</p>
<p>'How <hi rend="italic">did</hi> you do it? You do not know how I
have thirsted to hear! Oh, <hi rend="italic">do</hi> tell me!'</p>
<p>Her hands are clasped together, and held up close under her chin,
which always gives<pb n="383     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> a greater air of
urgency. But in his eyes she reads no acquiescence, only a deep
embarrassment.</p>
<p>'Do not you think it is just as well sometimes not to know how the
strings are pulled?'</p>
<p>She is silenced for a moment, brought up against the dead wall of
his resistance; then persists:</p>
<p>'Surely you must understand of what profound interest it is to me
to learn how <hi rend="italic">you</hi> succeeded where <hi
rend="italic">I</hi> failed so egregiously.'</p>
<p>'I am afraid I cannot tell you.'</p>
<p>His face is so full of distress, and his tone so final, that she
has no choice but to yield. She turns away to the table, and in a
constrained voice offers him tea. As he takes it from her, he sees
that a far-reaching blush, extending from ear to ear, has swallowed
up her pallor.</p>
<p>She has interpreted his refusal after her own manner. The reason
why he is unable to give her the details of his interview with<pb
n="384     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> Faustina is because those details have
largely consisted in such shameful accusations against herself as have
never quite ceased their odious chime in her ears since the dreadful
hour when she first heard them. Her tone is stiff and changed when she
next speaks:</p>
<p>'You, at least, will not mind telling me--if you remember, you said
in your note that she was leaving London for awhile--whether she has
yet returned.'</p>
<p>'She has gone to America on a lecturing tour.'</p>
<p>'Gone to America!' She falls into her bamboo chair again, as if her
legs could not support the weight of such news, while a long sigh of
relief heaves her laces and lawns. 'And to think that I should have
lived to be glad that she is in another hemisphere!'</p>
<p>It is his turn to put a question:</p>
<p>'And Miss Delafield?'</p>
<p>'She has gone into the country with her<pb n="385     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> parents to celebrate her coming of age. My eldest brother
is staying with them.'</p>
<p>The news had given her a sensible pleasure when she heard it, but
she announces it now in a tone of the deepest dejection.</p>
<p>'But you are glad?'</p>
<p>'Oh yes--very glad!'</p>
<p>There is so little of the quality alluded to in Althea's voice or
words that he looks at her puzzled and chilled. Naturally unable to
follow the course of her thoughts, her change of weather vaguely
disheartens him; while the conviction to which his refusal to enter
into explanations has made her leap, that he has heard and believed
Faustina's calumnies, and that to them has been owing his delay in
seeking her, ties lead to the tongue which in many imaginary dialogues
since they parted has been so eagerly glib.</p>
<p>Since she has not given him leave to sit down, he remains standing
by her, hat in hand, while in the distance, across the bosky<pb
n="386     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> lawn, little groups of people are moving
in leisurely enjoyment. Among them  Althea detects the Boteler
pair. William has made Clare lean on his arm, as he is fond of doing
in public--a tiresome mode of announcement of his hopes of paternity,
which always make his sister-in-law very angry. She forgets to be
angry now, in the anxiety of gauging the likelihood of their turning
housewards. There seems to be no immediate fear of it as they are
talking to successive guests. Althea knows that Clare likes to be
near William when he is in company, both to act as a gentle drag upon
his sprightliness, and to hinder his asking after people's dead or
disgraced relations, as he has a well-meant, but uncomfortable, way of
doing.</p>
<p>After all, why should Althea desire their absence, seeing that
their presence would be no interruption, since she and Drake seem to
have absolutely nothing to say to each other? After a while it appears
that he has some<pb n="387     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>thing to say, and when
he can speak from beneath the douche of cold water that her manner had
poured over him, he says it:</p>
<p>'I should have been earlier to see you, only that--'</p>
<p>'Only that; what?'</p>
<p>He wonders why her voice should tremble so much; he does not know
that she is breathlessly asking herself with what words not grossly
insulting he can put the explanation of his repugnance to meet
her.</p>
<p>'Only that I thought it kinder to stay away.'</p>
<p>'<hi rend="italic">Kinder!</hi>'</p>
<p>How painfully she is reddening again!</p>
<p>'I thought that the sight of me must be odious to you.'</p>
<p>She says neither 'Yes' nor ' No.'</p>
<p>'It was not my fault; but I knew that to you I must be associated
with the most painful and repulsive experience of your life.'</p>
<p>He gets no contradiction.</p><pb n="388     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I see by your silence that I was right, and so, as I say, I
thought it kinder not to thrust myself upon you till those
associations had had time to weaken. If I had been really kind, I
suppose I should have kept away altogether; but I was not quite up to
that.'</p>
<p>She listens in apparently acquiescent dumbness; and, after waiting
vainly for any reassuring utterance from her, he adds, in a tone of
deeply-wounded feeling, and with what, though she is not looking at
him, she knows to be a comprehensive glance at her luxurious
surroundings:</p>
<p>'At all events, <hi rend="italic">now</hi> you have found your
right setting.'</p>
<p>There is something so unmistakably 'going' about the air that
accompanies this last phrase that she rouses herself, and, sitting
upright in her chair, with a hand on each wicker arm, lifts a
strangely-moved, indignant face towards him, speaking at last in an
intense low voice: </p><pb n="389     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'I cannot compliment you upon your penetration.'</p>
<p>'Are you implying that I am wrong? that you are not happy?'</p>
<p>'I am miserable.'</p>
<p>'Is it possible?'--with agitation--'and yet, when I first caught
sight of you, I had such an impression of perfect well-being--of the
right woman in the right place.'</p>
<p>'The right woman in the right place--lying in a wicker chair doing
nothing; that is all you think I am fit for.' He makes a sign of eager
denial; but she goes on: 'However, appearances are deceitful, as I
told you when you accused me of going to faint at my tea-party in
Canning Town.'</p>
<p>The mention of that stormy entertainment draws them at once nearer
together; and without waiting any longer for leave, he sits down
beside her.</p>
<p>'And you are <hi rend="italic">miserable?</hi>'</p>
<p>'I say it advisedly--I am miserable; not<pb n="390     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> only so, but I am the cause of misery in others.' She
reads such a refreshing incredulity of this last statement in her
listener's face that she sails on with a high courage: 'They took me
in when, on your advice, I threw myself upon their compassion; they
did their best to cheer and comfort me, and, in return, I am the viper
who has come out of the heat and stung them.' She is surprised herself
at the force and beauty of this metaphor; but he receives it only with
open-eyed amazement. 'I know that it is very tiresome to be made the
recipient of an unasked confidence; but it is, or seems, so long since
I have had anyone to talk to <hi rend="italic">&#x00E0; coeur
ouvert</hi>, that you must try to forgive me.'</p>
<p>He has an impression that her hand has half slidden out towards
him, but the intention remains only a sketch; and they are both
growing so upset that neither is quite sure about it.</p><pb
n="391     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> 
<p>'I was afraid that it would be some time before you got over such a
shock,' he says, in a tone of the gravest, kindest sympathy, his words
coming very unreadily, through the excess of his apprehension lest he
should happen upon any that might touch her on the raw. 'I know that
to you it was the loss, not only of the person you loved best in the
world, but of a creed.'</p>
<p>She gives a slow assent, head downbent, the toe of one little shoe
drawing restless designs on the floor; then, as if dissatisfied,
qualifies it:</p>
<p>'And yet, no; that does not cover the area of my unhappiness. I
could do without Faustina'--pronouncing her name very distinctly, to
show him how well she can manage it--'since I have learnt that she
never existed as I believed her to be. I could do without her, if only
I could find someone else to teach me how to set about rebuilding my
life; the bricks are there, if<pb n="392     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> only
some mason would show me how to lay them upon each other. Left to
myself, it will be but a jerry-built edifice.'</p>
<p>Her words, grown very low, though perfectly audible, die into
silence. She has addressed them, apparently, to the red tiles at her
feet. It seems to her a very long moment before he takes up her
challenge.</p>
<p>'I have not yet told you the chief motive that brought me here
to-day.'</p>
<p>She snatches a half-reproachful glance at him.</p>
<p>'I hoped that it was a friendly feeling for me. Had you any
other?'</p>
<p>He does not answer her directly.</p>
<p>'Do you remember,' he says slowly, 'that evening at Canning Town,
just before we parted--'</p>
<p>'Yes.'</p>
<p>'You said to me that if it were not for Faustina, and what you owed
her, you would be inclined to come among us for good.<pb n="393
DEAR FAUSTINA"/> What I have come here for to-day is to ask you
whether that was a passing impulse, or the expression of a lasting
desire.'</p>
<p>Her gray eyes have leapt up from the tiles to throw themselves into
his. That is at first the sole response he gets; but presently a
trembling sentence falters forth:</p>
<p>'Do not tantalize me. Is there any place--can any place for me be
found among you?'</p>
<p>'Have not you already suffered enough maltreatment at our
hands?'</p>
<p>They both laugh joyfully.</p>
<p>'Not nearly!'</p>
<p>A moment later a slight cloud obscures her brilliancy.</p>
<p>'But the question is, Can you find any sphere of work for me where
I shall not do you discredit? <hi rend="italic">You</hi> know how apt I am to bungle
everything I attempt.'</p>
<p>'I will risk it.'</p>
<p>Again eyes and smiles meet, and there is<pb n="394     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
a blissful pause. He is the first to become business-like.</p>
<p>'I need not say that I have tied you to nothing. I came here with
not very high hopes; but if you really care to cast in your lot with
us, there is a place waiting for you.'</p>
<p>'What sort of a place? Shall I be up to it? Shall I dare to
undertake it?'</p>
<p>'I think so. Do you know that we have at last got our Women's
Settlement on its feet?'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'It is open to <hi rend="italic">all</hi> women, and does not postulate a University
education.'</p>
<p>'No?'</p>
<p>'We have knocked three or four houses into one, and got our Lady
Principal, and started our classes, and have been for a week in
working order.'</p>
<p>'Yes?'</p>
<p>'The residents are boarded and lodged;<pb n="395     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> each has a little room of her own, and common sitting and
dining rooms; and each takes up a special branch of work.'</p>
<p>'Such as--'</p>
<p>'Such as nursing the poor in their own homes, teaching classes of
boys.'</p>
<p>She has been following him with breathless attention, but at the
enumeration of the two kinds of service he has instanced shakes her
head despondently.</p>
<p>'Should I be any good at either? I have grown to distrust myself so
utterly.'</p>
<p>'You must not be impatient. I have not reached your place among us
yet.' At that she cheers up again. 'You remember your special
girls--the ones who were turned off from their factory for giving
information to the inspector?'</p>
<p>'Of course I do!'</p>
<p>'If you recollect, you were very kind to them--dancing with them at
our social evenings--'</p><pb n="396     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>'You are not going to suggest that I shall set up as the D'Egville
of Canning Town?'</p>
<p>'Will not you let me unfold my plan before you begin'to pick holes
in it?' She lays her hand across her lips with a pretty gesture of
determined silence, and he goes on: 'What I came here to suggest was
that you should utilize a talent I know that you possess in their
behalf.'</p>
<p>She lifts her eyebrows incredulously, but in her voice is an eager
hope:</p>
<p>'Do I possess one?'</p>
<p>'I know that, like Desdemona, you are "delicate with your needle."'
</p>
<p>'Yes, that is the one thing I am not mediocre at.'</p>
<p>She looks to him with joyful alertness for further explanation.</p>
<p>'Well, then, you could render us really valuable help by getting
those girls together and starting a co-operative workroom.'</p><pb
n="397     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>
<p>There is a slight pause, but Althea's kindling look and genial
expansion reassure him as to its not being one of disapproval.</p>
<p>'I think you <hi rend="italic">have</hi> found something for me that I might venture to
undertake,' she says humbly, yet with confidence. 'How clever of you
to have hit upon my one gift! It has lain in a napkin so long--Faustina
could not bear the sight of a needle--that I hope it has not grown
rusty.'</p>
<p>'Let me give you a rough outline of my idea,' he says, a slight
conscientious misgiving at the unquestioning docility of her
acquiescence mingling with his relief and joy, 'that I may not feel I
am letting you in for what you do not understand.'</p>
<p>She makes a sign of eager assent.</p>
<p>'I thought that you might get them to come to one of the
class-rooms--they are out of work, and would be only too thankful; and
send out circulars to your friends, telling them at what rate you
would take<pb n="398     DEAR FAUSTINA"/> in every kind of needlework
and dressmaking.'</p>
<p>She gives him a nod of bright agreement, and he goes on:</p>
<p>'I am only giving you the idea in the rough, but I am sure that it
has the elements of success in it, as it would supply an already
badly-felt want.  I know that you could make something of it.'</p>
<p>'Do you really believe that I might? At all events, I shall be only
too thankful to try'--with a long sigh of relief. 'When may I
come?'</p>
<p>'The sooner the better.'</p>
<p>In the eagerness of their project and their proximity, both have
again risen.</p>
<p>'And if I get into difficulties--if I want advice--help--will you be
within reach to give it me?'</p>
<p>'I shall be close at hand; I live at the Men's Settlement.' </p>
<p>Both are silent for awhile, a delightful<pb n="399     DEAR
FAUSTINA"/> dawning sense of the unity of interest that is for the
future to connect their lives giving their spirits that sort of hush
that comes with the real dawn. It is Althea who first regains
tremulous speech:</p>
<p>'How recklessly I am adding to my debt of gratitude to you, which
was already far too big ever to be discharged!'</p>
<p>He answers her look with a tender fixity.</p>
<p>'And you have counted the cost? You will not regret all this?'</p>
<p>His eye takes in with a comprehensive look all the details of her
high civilization. She breaks into emotional laughter.</p>
<p>'The gown is Clare's; the shoes are Fanny's--I renounce them
all!'</p>
<p>'What are you renouncing?' cries William, appearing round an
unexpected corner, with his wife still leaning on his unnecessary
arm, and looking curiously at Althea's unknown companion, while he
adds, in a fine vein of flat pleasantry: 'What are you renouncing<pb
n="400     DEAR FAUSTINA"/>--your godfathers and godmothers? Is not it
rather late in the day to do that?'</p>
<p>She turns upon him with a radiant smile.</p>
<p>'Not my godfathers and godmothers--but my
brother-in-law!'</p></div>


THE END.
